The Vain Oath (Yamin Laghw): No Sin, No Expiation
Discussion of vain oath: no sin, intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the vain oath, explain the Hanafi ruling that it incurs no sin and no expiation, and describe the intra-Hanafi nuance regarding Imam Muhammad's cautious view.

Core Idea
The vain oath is unintentional — Allah does not hold us accountable, so there is no sin and no expiation.

This lesson covers the vain oath (yamin laghw) — an oath uttered without intent, such as a slip of the tongue or a mistaken belief. The Quran explicitly removes accountability. While the matn author asserts no sin decisively, Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan expressed hope rather than certainty, leading to a refined intra-Hanafi discussion. The lesson shows how the decisive view prevails due to clear proof.

Guided Path
1
Define the vain oath
Identify it as an oath made without genuine intent — either a slip of the tongue, swearing mistakenly about something believed true, or swearing to do something permissible but not meaning it.
It differs from the false oath (deliberate lie) and binding oath (future intent).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The vain oath incurs no sin and no expiation. The Quran (2:225) explicitly says Allah does not hold people accountable for such oaths.
3
Cite the Quranic evidence
Quran 2:225: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for vain oaths.' This is decisive (qat'i) in meaning for this category.
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi nuance
Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan categorized oaths into three: expiated, non-expiated, and one he hoped Allah would not hold accountable – the vain oath. He suspended judgment, causing scholarly debate.
The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq asserts no sin decisively, unlike Imam Muhammad's cautious hope.
5
Understand the resolution
Later scholars (e.g., in Fath al-Qadir) affirmed that the proof is decisive, and the author's assertion (jism) is the reliable position. Imam Muhammad's hesitation was due to differing interpretations of 'laghw', but the correct view is no accountability.
The author's approach is preferred because the evidence is qat'i al-dalalah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Discussion of 'Verily deeds are by intentions.' And he only sins in the first [false oath] due to the hadith of Ibn Hibban raised: 'Whoever swears an oath in which he is sinful to cut off the property of a Muslim, Allah makes Paradise forbidden for him and enters him into the Fire.' And in the Two Sahihs: he meets Allah while He is angry with him. And in Sunan Abi Dawud: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever swears a binding oath falsely, let him take his seat in the Fire.' The meaning of 'binding' is enforced by the court, i.e., constrained because it is bound; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And better is to use the hadith of Bukhari from Abdullah ibn Umar from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'The major sins are associating partners with Allah, disobedience to parents, killing a soul, and the false oath.' For it is more general than whether one cuts off property of a Muslim or not. And it is stated in Ghayat al-Bayan and others that the false oath is a major sin, and it is as we mentioned, and it should be a major sin if one cuts off property of a Muslim or harms him, and becomes a minor sin if no harm results. And he does not sin in the second [vain oath] due to Allah's statement: 'Allah does not hold you accountable for vain oaths' [2:225]. Hence the author asserted no sin in the vain oath, but Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan did not assert it but conditioned it on hope, saying: Oaths are three: an expiated oath, a non-expiated oath, and an oath for which we hope Allah will not hold its swearer accountable. Then it was objected: How can he condition it on hope when it is certain? So the scholars differed in the answer. In al-Hidayah: He conditioned it on hope due to difference in its interpretation. But this was criticized in Fath al-Qadir because the correct is that the vain oath according to the first two interpretations and also the third is agreed upon that there is no punishment in the afterlife, and likewise in this world by expiation, so the excuse for conditioning on hope is incomplete. So the correct is what is said: that he did not intend conditioning but rather blessing by the name of Allah and politeness, like his (peace be upon him) statement to the people of graves: 'And we, if Allah wills, will join you.' As for the fourth interpretation, it is not well-known, and that it is vain is the choice of Sa'id. He meant by the first two interpretations our interpretation and that of al-Shafi'i, and by the third what is from al-Sha'bi and Masruq: besides the oath, one swears on sin and his oath becomes vain, and by the fourth the saying of Sa'id that he forbids himself what Allah has permitted of speech or action. The conclusion is that the first opinion is to assert clearly as the author did because of the decisive evidence, like asserting in similar matters that have difference in meaning.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the types of oaths, focusing on the false oath (yamin ghamus) and the vain oath (yamin laghw). The author begins by citing a hadith from Ibn Hibban that emphasizes the severity of swearing falsely to seize a Muslim's property—such a person is threatened with Hell. Another hadith from Bukhari lists the false oath as one of the major sins (kaba'ir), which is broader than just property-related harm. The commentary clarifies that the false oath is a major sin if it causes harm or seizes property; otherwise it may be minor. The second type, the vain oath, is derived from the Qur'anic verse (2:225) which removes accountability. Imam Abu Hanifa's student, Imam Muhammad, adds nuance by categorizing oaths into three: one requiring expiation, one not, and one he hopes Allah will not hold accountable—this latter he suspends in hope because of scholarly disagreement on the interpretation of 'laghw'. The passage resolves that the author's clear assertion (jism) is preferable due to decisive proof.

Key Terms
اليمين اللغو (al-yamin al-laghw): An oath made without intent, such as a slip of the tongue or swearing mistakenly about something one believes true.
اليمين المكفرة (al-yamin al-mukaffara): An oath that requires expiation (binding oath broken).
اليمين غير المكفرة (al-yamin ghayr al-mukaffara): An oath not requiring expiation – could be false (major sin) or vain (no sin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is true about the vain oath (yamin laghw)?
A) It is a major sin and requires expiation
B) It incurs no sin but requires expiation
C) It incurs no sin and no expiation
D) It is a minor sin but no expiation

Key Takeaway
The vain oath, defined by lack of intent, carries no legal consequence; the Hanafi school relies on the clear Quranic text for this ruling.

The Binding Oath: Expiation Without Sin? Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Clear legal ruling with inter-madhhab dispute and proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the binding oath (yamin mun'aqida) and contrast it with the Shafi'i view regarding expiation for false oaths, citing the key dalil from the text.

Core Idea
A binding oath requires expiation but no sin; a false oath is a major sin with no expiation in Hanafi fiqh.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on the binding oath (al-yamin al-mun'aqida) – an oath about a future event. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq emphasizes that breaking it entails expiation (kaffarah) and nothing else; no sin attaches. This leads to a sharp disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who obligates expiation even for false oaths (al-yamin al-ghamus). We will examine the proofs and the reasoning behind the Hanafi position, including the use of a hadith from Imam Ahmad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the author is ruling upon: the binding oath on a future event. The ruling is that only expiation (kaffarah) is due upon breaking it – no sin.
The author emphasises 'only' (faghat) to exclude false oaths from expiation.
2
Analyse the objection and refutation
Consider the objection from al-Tabyin: if expiation covers sin, then the binding oath must involve sin. Follow the two-fold refutation: the word 'only' distinguishes binding from false oaths; and expiation is an act of worship that cannot remove a major sin.
The false oath is a pure major sin (kabirah), while the binding oath is permissible (mubah).
3
Map the inter-madhhab dispute
State the Shafi'i view: expiation is required for false oaths because they violate Allah's sanctity. Then articulate the Hanafi response: expiation is only for minor sins or permissible acts; a major sin cannot be expiated by kaffarah.
The key proof for Hanafis is a hadith listing five things with no expiation, including a false oath seizing property.
4
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafis require expiation only for binding oaths, Shafi'is also for false oaths. This affects whether a person who breaks a false oath must feed the poor or fast.
The dispute is legitimate (ikhtilaf) and both schools have strong proofs.
5
Reflect on the epistemological weight
Recognise that the Hanafi position is from zahir al-riwayah and is relied upon (mufta bihi). The hadith from Ahmad is a strong dalil, but the Shafi'i objection is based on qiyas.
The author argues the Hanafi view is the most sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قوله : (وعلى آت منعقدة وفيها كفارة فقط) أي حلفه على آتِ تسمى منعقدة نفياً كان أو إثباتاً وحكمها وجوب الكفارة إذا حنث لقوله تعالى ولكن يؤاخذكم بما عقدتم الأيمان فكفارته ﴾ [المائدة: ۸۹] الآية والمراد منها اليمين في المستقبل بدليل قوله تعالى واحفظوا أيمانكم [المائدة: ٨٩] ولا يتصور الحفظ عن الحنث والهتك إلا في المستقبل. وقد اعترض في التبيين على المصنف بأنه لا معنى لقوله «فقط» لأن في اليمين المنعقدة إثماً أيضاً ولفظ الكفارة ينبيء عنه لأن معناها الستارة وهي لا تجب إلا لرفع المأثم ا هـ. وهو مردود من وجهين: أحدهما أن معنى قوله «فقط» أنه لا كفارة في غيرها من الغموس بياناً لذلك خلافاً للشافعي فإنه أوجب الكفارة في الغموس كالمنعقدة لأنها شرعت لدفع ذنب هتك حرمة اسم تعالى، وقد تحقق بالاستشهاد بالله كاذباً فأشبه المعقودة ولنا أنها كبيرة محضة والكفارة عبادة حتى تتأدى بالصوم ويشترط فيها النية فلا تناط بها بخلاف المعقودة فإنها مباحة، ولو كان فيها ذنب فهو متأخر متعلق باختيار مبتدأ وما في الغموس ملازم فيمتنع الالحاق ؛ كذا في الهداية. وذكر في فتح القدير أن المعقودة عند الشافعي ليست سوء المكسوبة بالقلب، وكون الغموس قارنها الحنث لا ينفي الانعقاد عنده، وكونها لا تسمى يميناً لأنها لم تنعقد للبر بعيد إذ لا شك في تسميتها يميناً لغة وعرفاً وشرعاً بحيث لا يقبل التشكيك، فليس الوجه إلا ما قدمناه أن من شرعية الكفارة لدفع ذنب أصغر لا يستلزم شرعها لدفع ذنب أكبر، وإذا أدخلها في مسمى المنعقدة وجعل المنعقدة تنقسم إلى غموس وغيرها عسر النظر معه إلا أن يكون لغة أو سمع وقد روى الإمام أحمد في مسنده بإسناد جيد عن النبي عليه السلام في حديث مطول قال فيه خمس ليس فيهن كفارة الشرك بالله، وقتل النفس بغير حق، وبهت المؤمن والفرار من الزحف ويمين صابرة يقتطع بها مال مسلم بغير

المعاقبة في الآخرة، وقيل هي المؤاخذة بالكفارة؛ كذا في الكشاف وغيره. والثاني أظهر بدليل ما بعده، ولا شك أن تفسير اللغو على رأينا ليس أمراً مقطوعاً به إذا الشافعي قائل بأن هذا من جرم علقه بالرجاء وهذا معنى دقيق ولم أر من عرج عليه .اهـ. ونظر بعضهم فيه بأن خلاف الشافعي بعد محمد فكيف يقال إن محمداً علقه بالرجا باعتباره وحينئذ فلا محيص عما قاله المحقق ابن الهمام اهـ. فالأنسب أن يقول في النهر كما قال بعض الفضلاء فحيث كان المنفي المؤاخذة بالكفارة كان اللغو بالنظر إلى حكم الآخرة مسكوناً عنه في الآية فلا نص عليه فلذا علقه بالرجاء، وقد يقال أيضاً : إن اجتهاد الإمام محمد بأن اللغو هو : كذا ليس قطعياً نافياً لاجتهاد غيره بخلافه فحيث كان ما قاله محمد مبنياً على ظنه أنه هو اللغو لم يجزم بحكمه لاحتمال أن اللغو غيره تأمل قوله : (ناقض نفسه
Allah. His statement: 'And regarding a future [oath], it is binding and in it only expiation.' That is, his oath about a future matter is called binding, whether negative or affirmative, and its ruling is the obligation of expiation if he breaks it, due to Allah's statement: 'But Allah will hold you accountable for what you have bound in oaths, so its expiation...' [5:89] and the verse intends the future oath, evidence being His statement 'and keep your oaths' [5:89], and preservation from breaking and violation is only conceivable in the future. An objection was raised in al-Tabyin against the author: there is no meaning to his saying 'only' because in the binding oath there is also sin, and the word expiation indicates that, for its meaning is covering, and it is not obligatory except to remove sin. It is refuted from two aspects: one is that his saying 'only' means that there is no expiation in other [oaths] like the false [oath], clarifying that, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he made expiation obligatory in the false oath like the binding, because it is legislated to repel the sin of violating the sanctity of Allah's name, and that is realized by swearing by Allah falsely, so it resembles the binding. But our [Hanafi] position is that it [false oath] is a pure major sin, and expiation is an act of worship, even performed by fasting, and intention is required, so it is not attached to it, unlike the binding oath which is permissible. Even if there were sin in it, it is delayed and depends on a new choice, while what is in the false oath is inherent, so analogy is prevented; thus in al-Hidayah. And in Fath al-Qadir it is mentioned that the binding oath according to al-Shafi'i is not evil earned by the heart, and that the false oath has breaking coinciding with it does not negate its binding in his view, and that it is not called an oath because it did not bind to righteousness is far-fetched, for there is no doubt it is called an oath linguistically, customarily, and legally beyond doubt. So the only point is what we presented: that the legislation of expiation to repel a minor sin does not entail its legislation to repel a major sin. And if he [al-Shafi'i] includes it in the definition of binding and makes binding divide into false and others, the discussion becomes difficult with him unless linguistically or by hearing. Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated in his Musnad with a good chain from the Prophet (peace be upon him) in a long hadith: 'Five have no expiation: associating partners with Allah, killing a soul unjustly, slandering a believer, fleeing from battle, and a binding oath by which one cuts off the property of a Muslim without [right].' So, the binding oath? Punishment in the afterlife, and it is said it is accountability by expiation; thus in al-Kashshaf and others. The second is more apparent by evidence after it, and there is no doubt that the interpretation of laghw according to our view is not a cut-and-dried matter, since al-Shafi'i says this is of sin; he conditioned it on hope, and this is a subtle meaning I have not seen anyone explore. Some criticized this: the disagreement of al-Shafi'i is after Muhammad, so how can it be said that Muhammad conditioned it on hope considering him? Then there is no escape from what the investigator Ibn al-Humam said. So it is more appropriate to say in al-Nahr as some virtuous said: since what is negated is accountability by expiation, laghw with regard to the afterlife ruling is silent in the verse, so there is no text on it, therefore he conditioned it on hope. It can also be said: Muhammad's ijtihad that laghw is such is not definitive negating the ijtihad of others to the contrary, so since what Muhammad said was based on his opinion that it is laghw, he did not assert its ruling due to the possibility that laghw is something else. Consider his statement: 'He contradicts himself.'
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This section from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the binding oath (yamin mun'aqida), which is an oath concerning a future event. The core ruling is that breaking it requires expiation (kaffarah) only, and no sin is incurred. The author emphasizes the word 'only' (faghat) to distinguish it from the false oath, which does not require expiation in the Hanafi school. An objection is noted: if expiation removes sin, then the binding oath must also involve sin. The refutation explains that expiation in the binding oath is not for sin but for breaking a permissible oath; the false oath, being a major sin (kabirah), cannot be expiated by kaffarah, which is an act of worship. The passage then contrasts the Shafi'i position, which requires expiation even for false oaths, and argues that expiation is only for minor sin, not major. Later, it returns to the debate about the vain oath (laghw), mentioning that even Imam Muhammad's suspension was not decisive because of the ijtihad difference. The end is fragmented but discusses the punishment or accountability in the afterlife.

Key Terms
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqida): An oath concerning a future event, sworn with intent, binding such that breaking it requires expiation.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; an act of worship to cover the breach of an oath, such as feeding the poor, clothing, or fasting.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamin al-ghamus): A false oath sworn on a past or present event, deliberately lying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a false oath (yamin ghamus)?
A) It requires expiation and repentance.
B) It requires expiation only.
C) It is a major sin with no expiation.
D) It is not considered an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a binding oath (future) requires expiation but no sin, while a false oath (past/present) is a major sin with no expiation—a distinction rooted in a hadith from Ahmad.

The Basis of Kaffarah: Sin Not Required
Arguing that kaffarah is due even without sin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that kaffarah is due upon breaking a valid oath regardless of sin, and apply the qiyas with travel to explain this ruling.

Core Idea
Kaffarah in oaths is triggered by the act of breaking, not by sin — just as qasr in prayer is triggered by travel, not hardship.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author refutes the claim that kaffarah (expiation) for a broken oath is only due when a sin is committed. He argues that breaking the oath (al-hinth) itself is the operative cause (sabab) for kaffarah, regardless of sin. The proof is a qiyas with the ruling on shortening prayer (qasr), which is based on the fact of travel, not on the hardship experienced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Read the passage and note the opponent's assertion: that kaffarah is only a covering for sin, implying that if no sin, no kaffarah.
The opponent is unnamed but represents a view the author disagrees with.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
The author states that sin is not necessarily attached to a valid oath (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah). Breaking the oath may even be obligatory or recommended, so it cannot be inherently sinful.
This is the key claim: the ruling is detached from the moral status.
3
Examine the internal contradiction
The author points out that the same opponent, a little later, accepts that if a swearer breaks the oath while unconscious or insane, he still breaks the oath and kaffarah is incurred. Yet there is no sin in such a case.
This shows the opponent's inconsistency.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
The author draws an analogy: just as the ruling on shortening prayer (qasr) is based on the fact of travel (safar), not on the reality of hardship (mashaqqa), so the ruling on kaffarah is based on the evidence (al-dalil) which is breaking the oath (al-hinth), not on the reality of sin.
The qiyas uses a well-established fiqh principle to prove that the operative cause (illa) is the act itself, not its underlying wisdom.
5
State the conclusion
Thus, it is established that kaffarah is due even when there is no sin at all. The purpose of kaffarah is not necessarily to cover sin; it is an obligation that arises from the act of breaking a valid oath.
This is the final Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيهما أن الإثم ليس لازماً للمنعقدة بل قد يكون الحنث واجباً وقد يكون مستحباً فلم يصح إطلاقه كما لا يخفى والعجب منه أنه بعد يسير ناقض نفسه بأن قال: لو فعله الحالف وهو مغمى عليه أو مجنون فإنه يحنث لتحقق الشرط حقيقة، ولو كانت الحكمة رفع فالحكم يدار على دليله وهو الحنث لا على حقيقة الذنب كما أدبر الحكم على السفر لا على حقيقة المشقة ا هـ. فقد علم أنه لا يلزم في الكفارة أن تكون ستارة للذنب بل تجب ولا ذنب أصلاً.
Second: that sin is not necessarily attached to the valid oath, rather breaking the oath may be obligatory and may be recommended, so it is not correct to say it is always sinful, as is not hidden. And it is surprising that he, a little later, contradicts himself by saying: 'If the swearer does it while unconscious or insane, he breaks the oath because the condition is realized truly, and if the wisdom was to remove sin, then the ruling is based on its evidence, which is breaking the oath, not on the reality of sin, just as the ruling on travel is based on travel, not on the reality of hardship.' End quote. Thus it is known that it is not necessary for kaffarah to be a covering for sin, rather it is due even when there is no sin at all.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the issue of sin and expiation in oaths. The author argues that sin is not a necessary condition for a valid oath (yamin mun'aqidah). He responds to an opponent who claimed that the purpose of kaffarah is to cover sin, hence if there is no sin, no kaffarah is due. The author points out a contradiction: the same opponent accepts that if one breaks the oath while unconscious or insane, he still breaks the oath and incurs kaffarah, even though there is no sin. Therefore, the ruling revolves around the actual breaking of the oath (al-hinth), not on the presence of sin. Kaffarah is wajib even without sin.

Key Terms
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah): A valid oath that creates a legal obligation; if broken, kaffarah is due.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for breaking a valid oath; includes feeding or clothing ten poor people, or freeing a slave.
الحنث (al-hinth): The act of breaking an oath by doing what was sworn not to do or vice versa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for the obligation of kaffarah in a valid oath?
A) The presence of sin
B) The occurrence of breaking the oath (al-hinth)
C) The intention when swearing
D) The hardship caused

Key Takeaway
The obligation of kaffarah depends on the act of breaking a valid oath, not on the presence of sin.

Kaffarah for Oaths Under Compulsion or Forgetfulness
Establishing kaffarah for oaths under compulsion or forgetfulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on kaffarah for oaths made under compulsion or forgetfulness and evaluate the counter-argument from Fath al-Qadir.

Core Idea
An oath made under compulsion or forgetfulness is still binding and breaking it requires kaffarah.

This lesson examines a key debate within the Hanafi school: whether an oath uttered under compulsion or forgetfulness incurs kaffarah upon breaking. The majority Hanafi position relies on the prophetic hadith that oaths, like marriage and divorce, are serious even in jest. However, a critical view from Fath al-Qadir challenges this analogy, arguing that the hadith only applies to jokers, not to those who are forgetful or mistaken. We will dissect the arguments and understand the supported ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its proof
Identify the ruling: kaffarah is due for breaking an oath even if made under compulsion or forgetfulness. The primary proof is the hadith: 'Three things are serious whether in earnest or jest: marriage, divorce, and oaths.'
The Hanafi scholars argue that the oath is valid (mun'aqidah) and thus breaking it triggers kaffarah.
2
Identify the opposing critique and its basis
The scholars of Fath al-Qadir object that the hadith only mentions the joker (hazi), who intends the utterance but not its consequences. The forgetful person (nasi) or mistaken person (mukhti) never intended to swear, so the hadith does not apply to them. They also analogize to laghw (vain oaths), which have no ruling.
The opposing view holds that such oaths are not binding and no kaffarah is required.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi authors respond by defining 'forgetful' broadly to include those who utter heedlessly, and they argue that the forgetful person is comparable to the joker in lacking full intent. They also note that the hadith is general and that other evidence supports binding oaths even when uttered unintentionally.
Various definitions of nasi are presented, including the mistaken and the heedless, to include those who did not intend the oath.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under the Hanafi position, one who breaks an oath made under compulsion or forgetfulness must perform kaffarah (feeding ten poor people, clothing them, or fasting three days). Under the opposing view, no expiation is required.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو مكرهاً أو ناسياً أي في المنعقدة كفارة إذا حنث ولو كان حلف مكرهاً أو ناسياً لقوله عليه السلام ثلاث جدهن جد وهزلهن جد النكاح والطلاق واليمين». كذا استدل مشايخنا، وتعقبهم في فتح القدير بأنه لو ثبت حديث اليمين لم يكن فيه دليل لأن المذكور فيه جعل الهزل باليمين جداً والهازل قاصد لليمين غير راض بحكمه فلا يعتبر عدم رضاه به شرعاً بعد مباشرة السبب مختاراً، والناسي بالتفسير المذكور لم يقصد شيئاً أصلاً ولم يدر ما صنع، وكذا المخطئ لم يقصد قط التلفظ به بل بشيء آخر فلا يكون الوارد في الهازل وارداً في الناسي الذي لم يقصد قط مباشرة السبب فلا يثبت في حقه نصاً ولا قياساً. وإذا كان اللغو بتفسيرهم وهو أن يقصد اليمين مع ظن البر ليس لها حكم اليمين فما لم يقصده أصلاً بل هو كالنائم يجري على لسانه طلاق أو إعتاق لا حكم له أولى أن لا يكون له حكم اليمين. وأيضاً فتفسير اللغو المذكور في حديث عائشة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه وهو كلام الرجل في بيته كلا والله وبلى والله وإن لم يكن هو نفس التفسير الذي فسروا به الناسي فإن المتكلم كذلك في بيته لا يقصد التكلم به بل يجري على لسانه بحكم العادة غير مراد لفظه ولا معناه كان أقرب إليه من الهازل فحمل الناسي على اللاغي بالتفسير المذكور أولى من حمله على الهازل، وهو الذي أدينه وتقدم لنا مثله في الطلاق غافلاً ا هـ . وفي التبيين والمراد بالناسي المخطىء كما إذا أراد أن يقول اسقني الماء فقال والله لا أشرب الماء. وذكر في الكافي أنه المذهول عن بأن قيل له ألا تأتينا فقال بلى والله غير قاصد لليمين وإنما ألجأنا إلى هذا التأويل لأن حقيقة النسيان في اليمين لا تتصور هـ. وذكر الشمني أن حقيقته متصورة بأن حلف أن لا يحلف فنسي فحلف ا هـ . وهو مردود لأنه فعل المحلوف عليه ناسياً لا أن حلفه كان التلفظ به بأن قال الخ) أجاب في النهر بأن المدعي أن في المنعقدة إثماً وتخلفه فيما ذكر لعارض فلا يرد قوله : تلفظ والناسي بالتفسير (المذكور المراد به التفسير الآتي في قوله وفي فتح القدير والناسي هو : باليمين ذاهلاً الخ فكان المناسب تقديمه قوله : (وهو مردود الخ قال في النهر : فيه نظر إذ فعل (1) رواه أحمد في مسنده (٣٦٢/٢).
His saying: (even if compelled or forgetful) meaning in a valid oath, kaffarah is due when he breaks it, even if he swore while compelled or forgetful, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'Three things are serious whether in earnest or jest: marriage, divorce, and oaths.' This is how our scholars argued. But they were criticized in Fath al-Qadir because if the hadith of oaths is established, it does not contain proof, because what is mentioned in it is making jest in oaths serious, and the joker intends the oath but is not satisfied with its ruling, so his dissatisfaction is not considered legally after directly causing the cause voluntarily. The forgetful person, according to the mentioned interpretation, intended nothing at all and did not know what he did, and likewise the mistaken person never intended to utter it but rather something else, so what is mentioned about the joker does not apply to the forgetful person who never intended to directly cause the cause, so it is not established in his favor by text or analogy. If laghw according to their interpretation—that one intends the oath while thinking he will fulfill it—has no ruling of an oath, then what he did not intend at all, rather like a sleeper upon whose tongue divorce or emancipation runs, has no ruling, even more so it should have no ruling of an oath. Also, the interpretation of laghw mentioned in the hadith of Aisha from the Prophet, peace be upon him, that it is a man's speech in his house, 'No, by Allah' and 'Yes, by Allah,' even if it is not the same interpretation with which they interpreted the forgetful person, because the speaker in his house likewise does not intend to speak it but it runs on his tongue by habit, not intending its wording nor its meaning, was closer to him than the joker. So comparing the forgetful person to the laghi by the mentioned interpretation is more appropriate than comparing him to the joker, and that is what I follow, and we have a precedent for it in divorce while heedless. End quote. In al-Tabyin: the intended meaning of forgetful is the mistaken person, as when he wanted to say 'Give me water to drink' and he said 'By Allah, I will not drink water.' It was mentioned in al-Kafi that he is the one heedless about it, like when one said to him 'Will you not come to us?' and he said 'Yes, by Allah' without intending an oath, and we were forced to this interpretation because the reality of forgetfulness in oaths cannot be conceived. End quote. And al-Shumni mentioned that its reality can be conceived as when one swears that he will not swear, then forgets and swears. End quote. And it is rejected because he did the sworn action forgetfully, not that his swearing was by uttering it... He answered in al-Nahr that the claimant said that in the valid oath there is sin, and its absence in what was mentioned is due to an impediment, so his saying does not refute...
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage is an extended inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of kaffarah for oaths made under compulsion or forgetfulness. The Hanafi position, based on the hadith 'Three things are serious...', holds that such oaths are still binding and breaking them requires kaffarah. However, the critic from Fath al-Qadir objects that the hadith only applies to jokers, not to those who are forgetful or mistaken, as they had no intention of swearing. He argues that analogizing to laghw (vain oaths) which have no ruling, forgetful utterence should also have no ruling. The author replies by presenting various definitions of 'forgetful' (nasi) from different sources, distinguishing between one who utters an oath heedlessly and one who acts forgetfully. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is upheld, though there is debate about the precise definition.

Key Terms
الناسي (al-nasi): The forgetful person; in this context, one who utters an oath without intending it, or one who breaks an oath forgetfully.
الهازل (al-hazi): One who jokes; i.e., utters an oath in jest, intending the words but not the consequences.
اللغو (al-laghw): Frivolous or vain oath; an oath made inadvertently without serious intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for requiring kaffarah even when an oath is made under compulsion or forgetfulness?
A) The hadith 'Actions are by intentions'
B) The hadith 'Three things are serious whether in earnest or jest'
C) Qiyas on divorce
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that an oath made under compulsion or forgetfulness is still binding and requires kaffarah upon breaking, despite the counter-argument from Fath al-Qadir.

Breaking an Oath Under Duress or Forgetfulness
Ruling on hinth under duress or forgetfulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that breaking an oath under compulsion or forgetfulness still constitutes hinth, and identify the exception for purely involuntary actions.

Core Idea
The actual action counts as hinth even under duress; only total involuntariness exempts.

An oath is sacred, but what happens when you are forced to break it or forget? The Hanafi school holds that the legal effect of breaking an oath (hinth) depends on the actual performance of the sworn action, not on the presence of sin. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that compulsion or forgetfulness does not nullify the action – you still broke the oath and must pay kaffarah, unless the action was entirely outside your control, like water being poured down your throat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the opening: 'أو حنث كذلك أي مكرهاً أو ناسياً'. The author states that breaking the oath under compulsion or forgetfulness is still considered hinth (breach).
The ruling applies to both forgetting and being forced.
2
Understand the rationale
The text says: 'لأن الفعل الحقيقي لا ينعدم بالإكراه أو النسيان' – the actual action is not negated by compulsion or forgetfulness. The action physically occurred, so the condition for hinth is met.
Hanafi focus on the objective act, not the subjective state.
3
Distinguish condition (shart) from cause (sabab)
The author clarifies that 'shart' in the passage refers to 'sabab'. Breaking the oath is the cause (sabab) for kaffarah, not merely a condition. The action itself is the cause that triggers the obligation.
This is a technical usul point: cause vs condition.
4
Apply the exception
If water is poured into the throat under compulsion, it is not considered hinth because the person did not perform the action (drinking). But if the person voluntarily holds the water and then drinks, it becomes hinth.
Key distinction: total involuntariness vs. minimal volition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو حنث كذلك) أي مكرهاً أو ناسياً لأن الفعل الحقيقي لا ينعدم بالإكراه أو النسيان وهو الشرط، وكذا إذا فعله وهو مغمى عليه أو مجنون لتحقق الشرط حقيقة، ولو كان الحكمة رفع الذنب فالحكم يدار على دليله وهو الحنث لا على حقيقته الذنب؛ كذا في الهداية. ومراده من الشرط السبب لأن الحنث عندنا سبب لوجوب الكفارة لا شرط كما سيأتي ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال : إن فعل المحلوف عليه شرط في الحنث والحنث سبب للكفارة إلا أن يقال : إن الحنث هو عين فعل المحلوف عليه فحينئذ يحتاج إلى التأويل. قيد بالحنث لأنه لو لم يحنث كما لو حلف أن لا يشرب فأوجر أو صب في حلقه الماء مكرهاً فإنه لا اعتبار به . وقيده قاضيخان بأن يدخل في جوفه بغير صنعه فلو صب في فيه وهو مكره فأمسكه ثم شربه بعد ذلك حنث ا هـ.
His saying: (or likewise breaks the oath) meaning under compulsion or forgetfully, because the actual action is not negated by compulsion or forgetfulness, and that is the condition. Similarly, if he does it while unconscious or insane, because the condition is realized truly. Even if the wisdom was to remove sin, the ruling is based on its evidence, which is the breaking of the oath, not on the reality of sin; so in al-Hidayah. And by 'condition' he means the cause, because according to us, breaking the oath is a cause for the obligation of kaffarah, not a condition, as will come; so in Fath al-Qadir. And it may be said: The action sworn against is a condition for the breaking of the oath, and the breaking is a cause for kaffarah, unless it is said that the breaking is the very action sworn against, in which case interpretation is needed. He restricted it to breaking the oath because if he does not break it, as if he swore not to drink and then he was forced to drink or water was poured into his throat under compulsion, then it is not considered. And Qadikhan restricted it to what enters his stomach without his action; if water is poured into his mouth while he is compelled and then he holds it and later drinks it, he breaks the oath.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement explaining that breaking an oath (al-hinth) is legally effective even if done under compulsion or forgetfulness. The author clarifies that the actual action (fi'l haqiqi) is not nullified by duress or forgetfulness; therefore, if the sworn action is performed in such states, it still constitutes a breach. He distinguishes between the condition (shart) and cause (sabab): breaking the oath is the cause for kaffarah, not a condition. The text also provides an example: if one swears not to drink and then someone pours water into his throat forcibly, it is not a valid breach; but if he voluntarily holds the water in his mouth and then swallows, it is.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Compulsion; being forced to perform an action against one's will.
الحنث (al-hinth): The act of breaking an oath by performing the sworn-forbidden action or omitting the sworn-required action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person swears not to drink and then water is poured into his throat under compulsion, does he break the oath?
A) Yes, and kaffarah is due
B) No, it is not considered a breach
C) Yes, but no kaffarah because of duress
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
Breaking an oath under compulsion or forgetfulness still counts as hinth and obligates kaffarah, unless the action was entirely involuntary.

Valid Formulations of Oaths in Hanafi Fiqh
Listing and explaining the valid formulations for binding oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the valid wordings for a binding oath in Hanafi Fiqh and distinguish which formulations require intention.

Core Idea
A binding oath must explicitly invoke Allah or His attributes; 'Bismillah' is not an oath unless intended.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq enumerates the formulations that constitute a valid oath (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah) in Hanafi Fiqh. Understanding these wordings is crucial because an oath that does not meet the required wording is not binding and requires no expiation. The text also addresses variations and differences of opinion within the school, especially regarding the phrase 'Bismillah' and the effect of customary usage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core valid wordings
List the explicit phrases given: 'by Allah', 'by al-Rahman', 'by al-Rahim', 'by His Majesty', 'by His Grandeur', 'I swear', 'I take an oath', 'I testify', 'by the life of Allah', 'by the covenant of Allah', 'by the pledge of Allah', 'upon a vow', 'a vow of Allah', and the conditional 'if I do such-and-such I am a disbeliever'.
These all involve direct reference to Allah or His attributes.
2
Analyze the exceptions
The text highlights that 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) is NOT an automatic oath; it requires intention. Also, the word 'Allah' alone (without a preposition) is considered an oath by some authorities, while others require the waw (و) or customary usage.
This shows that formal structures and intention interact.
3
Examine the role of custom (urf)
The passage mentions that among Christians of a certain region, the phrase 'Wasm Allah' (and by God) became customary as an oath. Custom can affect what counts as an oath.
Hanafi fiqh recognizes urf as a secondary source.
4
Consider the intra-Hanafi differences
There is a narration (riwayah) from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan through Ibn Rustom that 'Bismillah' is an oath absolutely, without need for intention. The author advises caution in fatwa.
The mufta bihi position is that intention is required.
5
Understand forgetfulness and breaking the oath
If someone forgetfully performs the sworn action, the oath is still valid and breaking it incurs kaffarah. The forgetfulness does not negate the oath itself.
This clarifies that the ruling focuses on the wording, not the mental state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (واليمين بالله تعالى والرحمن والرحيم وجلاله وكبريائه وأقسم وأحلف وأشهد وإن لم يقل بالله ولعمر الله وأيم الله وعهد الله وميثاقه وعلى نذر ونذر الله وإن فعل كذا فهو كافر بيان الألفاظ اليمين المنعقدة؛ فقوله بالله والرحمن والرحيم بيان للحلف باسم من أسمائه تعالى لأنه يعتقد تعظيم الله تعالى فصلح ذكره حاملاً أو مانعاً. وفي المجتبى : لو قال والله بغيرها كعادة الشطار فيمين. قلت: فعل هذا ما يستعمله الأتراك بالله بغير هاء فيمين أيضاً ا.هـ. بلفظه . وأفاد بعطف «الرحمن» على «الله» أن المراد بالله اللفظ. وقيد به احترازاً عن بسم الله فإنه ليس بيمين إلا أن ينويه. وفي المنتقى رواية ابن رستم عن محمد أنه يمين مطلقاً فليتأمل عند الفتوى. ولو قال الله يكون وبسم يميناً؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وفي فتح القدير : قال الله لأفعلن المختار أنه ليس بيمين لعدم التعارف وعلى هذا بالواو إلا أن نصارى ديارنا تعارفوه فيقولون واسم الله ا هـ. والظاهر أن الله يمين كما جزم به في البدائع معللاً لأن الاسم والمسمى واحد عند أهل السنة بسم والجماعة فكان الحلف بالاسم حلفاً بالذات كأنه قال بالله ا هـ والعرف لا اعتبار به في بسم المحلوف عليه ناسياً لا ينافي كونه يميناً بدليل أنه يكفر ،مرتين مرة باعتبار أنه فعل المحلوف عليه، وأخرى باعتبار حنثه في اليمين .اهـ. قال بعض الفضلاء أقول : الحق ما في البحر فإن فعل المحلوف عليه ناسياً وإن لم يناف كونه يميناً لكن تعلق النسيان به من جهة كونه حنثاً لا من جهة كونه يميناً إذ هو من هذه الجهة لم يتعلق به النسيان كما لا يخفى على منصف .
His saying: (And the oath by Allah Almighty, and by al-Rahman, and by al-Rahim, and by His Majesty, and by His Grandeur, and 'I swear', and 'I take an oath', and 'I testify', even if he does not say 'by Allah', and 'by the life of Allah', and 'by the covenant of Allah', and 'by the pledge of Allah', and 'upon a vow', and 'a vow of Allah', and 'if I do such-and-such I am a disbeliever') is an explanation of the wording of the valid oath. So his saying 'by Allah', 'al-Rahman', 'al-Rahim' are explanations of swearing by one of His names, because he believes in the greatness of Allah, so the mention is suitable as a constraint or a prevention. In al-Mujtaba: If he says 'wallah' without the 'h' as is the habit of scoundrels, it is an oath. I say: This is what the Turks use: 'allah' without the 'h', it is also an oath. End quote verbatim. And the conjunction of 'al-Rahman' with 'Allah' indicates that what is meant by 'Allah' is the word. And he restricted it to exclude 'bismillah' because it is not an oath unless he intends it. In al-Muntaqa, a narration from Ibn Rustom from Muhammad that it is an oath absolutely, so one should reflect when giving fatwa. And if he says 'Allah' it is an oath, and 'bismillah' is an oath; thus in al-Khulasa. And in Fath al-Qadir: The chosen position regarding 'قال الله لأفعلن' is that it is not an oath due to lack of common usage, and similarly with the waw, except that the Christians of our region have adopted it, so they say 'wasm Allah'. End quote. And the apparent view is that 'Allah' is an oath, as was decisively stated in al-Bada'i', reasoning that the name and the named are one according to Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah, so swearing by the name is swearing by the essence, as if he said 'by Allah'. End quote. And custom has no consideration regarding 'bismillah' for the sworn action while forgetful does not negate it being an oath, as evidence that he becomes an apostate twice: once for doing the sworn action, and another for breaking the oath. End quote. One virtuous person said: I say the truth is what is in al-Bahr, because doing the sworn action forgetfully does not negate it being an oath, but forgetfulness attaches to it as breaking the oath, not as an oath, because from that aspect no forgetfulness attaches to it, as is not hidden to a fair-minded person.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a definition/ruling passage that enumerates the valid wordings for a binding oath (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah). The author explains that oaths are valid when using specific words that denote swearing by Allah or His attributes, such as 'by Allah', 'by al-Rahman', etc., as well as verbs like 'I swear'. He discusses various formulations including those without the letter waw, and the word 'Allah' alone. He notes differences of opinion: some say 'Bismillah' is not an oath unless intended; others say it is always an oath. The passage also addresses whether custom (urf) affects the validity. Finally, a virtuous scholar comments that forgetfulness in the sworn action does not negate the oath itself, it only affects the breaking of the oath.

Key Terms
اليمين المنعقدة (al-yamin al-mun'aqidah): A valid, binding oath that requires kaffarah if broken.
بسم الله (bismillah): Literally 'In the name of Allah'; considered an oath only if intended as such.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT automatically considered a valid oath?
A) 'By Allah'
B) 'By al-Rahman'
C) 'Bismillah'
D) 'I swear'

Key Takeaway
A valid binding oath in Hanafi Fiqh requires explicit wording invoking Allah or His attributes, with intention needed for phrases like 'Bismillah'.

Ruling on Breaking an Oath Under Coercion or Forgetfully
Commentary on the matn phrase 'ناسياً أو حنث كذلك'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hanth under coercion/forgetfulness, explain the reasoning (talil), and apply it to new scenarios.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness or coercion does not cancel the oath; the physical act itself constitutes a break.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle: an oath is broken by the mere physical act, regardless of intent. The source passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that hanth (breaking) occurs even under coercion or forgetfulness, because the oath targets the act itself. Understanding this distinction is vital for correctly ruling on expiation (kafarah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State clearly: performing the sworn act under coercion or forgetfully constitutes hanth. The ruling is that hanth occurs and expiation becomes wajib, though the person is excused from sin.
2
Understand the talil (reasoning)
The proof is that the physical act (al-fi'l al-haqiqi) exists regardless of mental state. The oath is about preventing the act, not about intent. Hence, the condition for hanth is satisfied.
The passage explicitly says: 'because the actual act does not cease to exist due to coercion or forgetfulness, and it is the condition.'
3
Distinguish sabab from shart
The passage clarifies that hanth is a cause (sabab) for expiation, not a condition (shart) for the oath. The act of breaking triggers the obligation, but the oath's validity depends on other factors.
Refer to the quote: 'What he means by condition is the cause, because hanth with us is a cause for expiation, not a condition.'
4
Examine the exceptions
Not every involuntary act counts as hanth. For example, if water is poured into the throat without any action, or if one vomits, there is no hanth. But if after coercion one voluntarily swallows, hanth occurs.
Qadikhan's restriction: 'entering his stomach without his action' does not count.
5
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools require intent for hanth. The Hanafi school relies on the physical act. Understand the practical difference: in Hanafi, forgetfulness or coercion still obligates expiation; in other schools, it may not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ناسياً أو حنث كذلك. ناسياً. وفي فتح القدير : والناسي هو من تلفظ باليمين ذاهلاً عنه ثم تذكر أنه تلفظ به. وفي بعض النسخ الخاطىء وهو من أراد أن يتكلم بكلام غير الحلف فجرى على لسانه الحلف اهـ. وهو الظاهر كما لا يخفى. وفي الخانية : رجل حلف أن لا يفعل كذا فنسي حلف بالطلاق أو بالصوم قالوا لا شيء عليه إلا أن يتذكر ا هـ. أنه كيف قوله : (أو حنث كذلك) أي مكرهاً أو ناسياً لأن الفعل الحقيقي لا ينعدم بالإكراه أو النسيان وهو الشرط، وكذا إذا فعله وهو مغمى عليه أو مجنون لتحقق الشرط حقيقة، ولو كان الحكمة رفع الذنب فالحكم يدار على دليله وهو الحنث لا على حقيقته الذنب؛ كذا في الهداية. ومراده من الشرط السبب لأن الحنث عندنا سبب لوجوب الكفارة لا شرط كما سيأتي ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد يقال : إن فعل المحلوف عليه شرط في الحنث والحنث سبب للكفارة إلا أن يقال : إن الحنث هو عين فعل المحلوف عليه فحينئذ يحتاج إلى التأويل. قيد بالحنث لأنه لو لم يحنث كما لو حلف أن لا يشرب فأوجر أو صب في حلقه الماء مكرهاً فإنه لا اعتبار به . وقيده قاضيخان بأن يدخل في جوفه بغير صنعه فلو صب في فيه وهو مكره فأمسكه ثم شربه بعد ذلك حنث ا هـ. قال بعض الفضلاء أقول : الحق ما في البحر فإن فعل المحلوف عليه ناسياً وإن لم يناف كونه يميناً لكن تعلق النسيان به من جهة كونه حنثاً لا من جهة كونه يميناً إذ هو من هذه الجهة لم يتعلق به النسيان كما لا يخفى على منصف .
Forgetfully or he breaks it similarly. Forgetfully. In Fath al-Qadir: The forgetful is one who utters the oath while unaware, then remembers. In some copies: the mistaken is one who intended to speak something else but the oath slipped. That is apparent. In al-Khaniyya: A man swore not to do something, then forgot and swore by divorce or fasting; they said nothing is upon him unless he remembers. How is that? He said: (or he breaks it similarly) meaning under coercion or forgetfully, because the actual act does not cease to exist due to coercion or forgetfulness, and it is the condition. Similarly, if he does it unconscious or insane, the condition is realized. Even if the wisdom is to remove sin, the ruling is based on its evidence (hanth) not on its reality. As in al-Hidayah. What he means by condition is the cause, because hanth with us is a cause for expiation, not a condition. Some say the act of the thing sworn is a condition for hanth, and hanth is a cause for expiation; unless hanth is exactly the act, then interpretation is needed. He restricted it to hanth because if he did not break it, like he swore not to drink and was made to vomit or water poured into his throat under coercion, it is not considered. Qadikhan restricted it to entering his stomach without his action. If water was poured into his mouth while coerced, he held it, then drank it, he breaks it. Some virtuous ones said: I say the truth is in al-Bahr; doing the thing forgetfully does not negate the oath, but forgetfulness attaches to it as hanth, not as an oath.
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'ناسياً أو حنث كذلك' regarding the effect of forgetfulness (nasiyan) and coercion (mukrahan) on breaking an oath. It begins by defining the forgetful person (nasi) as one who utters the oath while unaware, and distinguishes him from the mistaken (khata') who intended something else. The key Hanafi ruling: performing the sworn-act under coercion or forgetfully still constitutes a break (hanth) because the physical act exists; the oath is not about sin but about the act. The author clarifies that hanth is a cause (sabab) for expiation, not a pre-condition. He also distinguishes cases where the act is purely involuntary (e.g., water poured into throat) from cases where there is subsequent voluntary action (e.g., holding and then drinking). The passage concludes with a note that forgetfulness does not affect the validity of the oath itself, only the element of hanth.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by committing the forbidden action.
ناسي (nasi): One who utters the oath while unaware of it.
مكره (mukrah): One compelled to act under coercion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to drink water and then forgets and drinks, what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) No hanth because of forgetfulness
B) Hanth but no expiation
C) Hanth occurs and expiation is wajib
D) The oath is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, breaking an oath (hanth) is determined by the physical act alone; forgetfulness or coercion does not prevent the obligation of expiation.

Valid Wordings for an Oath in Hanafi Fiqh
Enumeration and analysis of phrases that constitute a valid oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which phrases constitute a valid binding oath (yamin mun'aqida) in Hanafi fiqh and explain the roles of intention and custom in determining validity.

Core Idea
Swearing by any name of Allah forms a binding oath; custom does not override the textual authority of His names.

This lesson explores the various wordings that create a binding oath according to the Hanafi school. You will learn which phrases automatically form an oath and which require intention, and you will encounter a key internal debate about the phrase 'Allah' alone. Understanding these rules is essential for knowing when an oath has been taken and when expiation becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the valid oath phrases
From the text, list all the phrases explicitly mentioned as valid oaths: 'by Allah,' 'by al-Rahman,' 'by al-Rahim,' 'by His Glory,' 'by His Magnificence,' 'I swear,' 'I vow,' 'I testify,' 'by the life of Allah,' 'by the covenant of Allah,' 'by His pledge,' 'by a vow,' 'by the vow of Allah.' Note that 'Bismillah' is excluded without intention.
The phrase 'by Allah' covers variations like 'Wa Allah' and 'Allah' alone per the Hanafi view.
2
Distinguish the debate over 'Allah' alone
The text presents an ikhtilaf: Bishr al-Marisi and some later scholars (e.g., in Fath al-Qadir) argue that saying 'Allah' without 'wa' is not an oath due to lack of common usage ('urf). The Hanafi position (al-mu'tamad) holds it is an oath because the name and the named are one (al-ism wa al-musamma wahid). Custom is not considered when textual evidence is present.
This debate affects everyday scenarios: e.g., a person exclaims 'Allah, I will not do this' – according to the Hanafi school, this is a binding oath.
3
Analyze the role of intention and custom
The text clarifies that for primary names of Allah (Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim, etc.), no intention is needed – they are automatically oaths. For shared names (al-Hakim, al-Aziz, etc.) that can apply to creation, the Hanafi school also holds no intention is required unless the speaker specifically intends a created being. Custom ('urf) is generally not a valid basis to deny an oath when the name is clearly from Allah's names or attributes.
Some scholars (Bishr al-Marisi) relied on custom for 'al-Rahman,' but the Hanafi school rejects this because al-Rahman is a specific divine name.
4
Apply the dialogue scenario from al-Walwalji
The text presents a scenario: one person says 'Allah, do not do this' or 'Wa Allah, do this' and the respondent says 'Naim' (yes). The ruling depends on intentions: if both intend to swear, both are sworn; if the initiator intends to make the other swear and the respondent intends a promise, no oath occurs; if the initiator intends to make swear and the respondent intends to swear, only the respondent is sworn; if neither intends anything, the respondent is sworn when the phrase was 'Allah' (without wa), and the initiator is sworn when the phrase was 'Wa Allah.'
This shows how intention can modify the oath's binding effect in interactive contexts.

He said: (The oath is by Allah ta'ala, and al-Rahman, and al-Rahim, and His Glory, and His Magnificence, and I swear, and I vow, and I testify, even if he does not say 'by Allah', and by the life of Allah, and by the covenant of Allah, and by His pledge, and by a vow, and by the vow of Allah, and if he does such-and-such he is an infidel.) This is an explanation of the valid wording of oaths. His saying 'by Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim' is an explanation of swearing by a name of Allah ta'ala, because he believes in the greatness of Allah ta'ala, so it is suitable as a prohibitive or obligating mention. In al-Mujtaba: If he said 'Wa Allah' without an addition like the habit of ruffians, it is an oath. I say: This is what the Turks use: 'Allah' without 'ha' is also an oath. He indicated by connecting 'al-Rahman' to 'Allah' that what is meant by 'Allah' is the word. This is a restriction to avoid 'Bismillah', for it is not an oath unless he intends it. In al-Muntaqa, a narration of Ibn Rustom from Muhammad that it is an oath unconditionally; so one should reflect when giving fatwa. And if he says 'Allah' it becomes an oath, and 'Bism' is an oath; as in al-Khulasa. In Fath al-Qadir: 'Allah' for something, the chosen view is that it is not an oath due to lack of common usage; similarly with 'wa' except that the Christians of our region commonly say 'Wa Ism Allah'. It is apparent that 'Allah' is an oath, as decided in al-Bada'i, reasoned because the name and the named are one according to Ahl al-Sunnah, so swearing by the name is swearing by the essence, as if he said 'by Allah'. And custom is not considered in names as we have presented. And al-Walwalji mentioned: A man said to another 'Allah, do not do this' or said 'Wa Allah, do this' and the other said 'yes'. If the initiator intended to swear and the respondent intended to swear, then each is a swearer; if the initiator intended to make swear and the respondent intended promise, then nothing upon either; if the initiator intended to make swear and the respondent intended to swear, then the respondent is the swearer; if neither intended anything, then in 'Allah' the swearer is the respondent, and in 'Wa Allah' the swearer is the initiator. He stated generally that an oath by Allah ta'ala does not depend on intention nor on custom; rather it is an oath whether they are familiar with it or not. This is the apparent view of our school and the correct one as in al-Dhakhira, because custom is not considered when there is textual evidence. This refutes what is in al-Walwaljiyya that if one says 'Wa al-Rahman, I will not do this', if he intends the chapter (al-Fatiha) then it is not an oath, but if he intends Allah ta'ala then it is an oath. This distinction in al-Rahman is the statement of Bishr al-Marisi, while the school holds it is an oath without intention. Similarly swearing by 'Allah' is like swearing by 'the one who there is no god but He', 'Lord of the heavens and earth', 'Lord of the worlds', 'Master of the Day of Judgment', 'the First who there is nothing before Him', 'the Last who there is nothing after Him'. And he indicated by connecting 'al-Rahim' to 'al-Rahman' that it means among His names there are specific and shared ones like al-Hakim, al-Alim, al-Qadir, al-Aziz. The correct view is that it does not depend on intention, contrary to some mashayikh regarding shared names, because since it is used for Allah and for others, one of the two intentions is not determined except by intention. This was preferred in Ghayat al-Bayan but it is against the school because these names, even if used for creation, but the Creator is determined by the context of swearing, since swearing by other than Allah is not permissible, so the apparent is that he intended the name of Allah, unless he intended other than Allah, then it is not an oath because he intended what his words could bear. In al-Dhakhira and al-Walwaljiyya: If he says 'Wa al-Talib wa al-Ghalib, I will not do this', it is an oath, and it is the custom of the people of Baghdad. This does not indicate that being an oath depends on custom; rather after judging it to be an oath, he informed that the people of Baghdad customarily swear by it. This refutes what is in Fath al-Qadir that would require either considering custom for names not found in Quran and Sunnah, because 'al-Talib' is not specifically mentioned. Rather his saying: (and by that refuted what is in Fath al-Qadir...) al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say: first, the existing phrasing in al-Walwaljiyya is 'al-Talib al-Ghalib' without 'wa', indicating that it is one name; if it were with 'wa' it would be two oaths. Second, the investigator intended to prove that the mentioned phrase is among His names, but he found no proof except the verse indicating that 'Ghalib' is an attribute, so he combined it with 'al-Talib' to make it permissible as an oath, just as 'al-Awwal' who nothing is before him... (text cuts off)
Source pages p. 473

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn listing the valid wordings for an oath (yamin). It is a front matter for the book of oaths. The author states that swearing by the names of Allah (Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim, etc.) and by expressions like 'Aqsam' (I swear) constitute binding oaths. He addresses the debate over whether 'Allah' alone (without waw) is an oath: some like Bishr al-Marisi deny it, but the Hanafi school holds it is, because the name and the named are one. Custom is not considered when there is textual evidence. He also explains a scenario from al-Walwalji where two people are involved in an oath dialogue, detailing how intentions affect the ruling. The passage then discusses swearing by shared names (like al-Hakim) and concludes that they do not require intention, unless the speaker intends a created being. The final part deals with the phrase 'al-Talib wa al-Ghalib', confirming it as an oath among the people of Baghdad, and clarifies that this does not mean custom is the basis; rather, it is still considered an oath by the school, and al-Maqdisi refines the argument.

Key Terms
يمين منعقدة (yamin mun'aqida): A valid binding oath that triggers expiation if broken.
يمين بالله (yamin billah): Swearing by the name of Allah, which is the valid wording for an oath.
الاسم والمسمى واحد (al-ism wa al-musamma wahid): The name and the named are one — a theological principle used to argue that swearing by Allah's name is equivalent to swearing by Allah Himself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) والرحمن
C) بسم الله

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, swearing by any name of Allah or a recognized attribute constitutes a binding oath, and custom does not override the textual authority of these names.

How a Response to an Oath Becomes an Oath: The Role of Intention
Ruling on response to an oath creating an oath based on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a response to an oath constitutes a valid oath and identify the decisive factor of intention.

Core Idea
The response 'yes' to an oath becomes an oath only when both parties intend swearing.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario: when a person says 'Allah, do not do that' or 'By Allah, do that,' and the other replies 'Yes.' Does that 'Yes' create an oath? The answer depends on the intentions of both parties. We will analyze the Hanafi ruling on this based on the passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, which clarifies how intention determines the legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Exchange Forms
Notice the two wordings: 'Allah, do not do such-and-such' and 'Wallah, do such-and-such.' The ruling differs slightly depending on the wording when no intention is expressed.
The first uses the name 'Allah' alone; the second uses 'Wallah' (by Allah).
2
Analyze the Four Intention Combinations
The passage presents four scenarios: both intend to swear; initiator intends to make the other swear and respondent intends to promise; initiator intends to make swear and respondent intends to swear; neither intends anything. For each, determine who is bound by an oath.
The key is that 'na'am' (yes) repeats the oath wording when both intend swearing.
3
Extract the General Rule
From the four cases, derive that the formation of an oath depends on the overlapping intentions of the parties, not merely on the literal wording.
When neither intends, the wording determines the swearer: 'Allah' makes the responder the swearer; 'Wallah' makes the initiator the swearer.
4
Connect to the Principle of 'Al-Umur bi-Maqasidiha'
Realize that this ruling is an application of the maxim 'Acts are judged by intentions.' The response only becomes an oath when the intention to swear exists.
This is a fundamental Hanafi principle that runs through many areas of fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر الولوالجي رجل قال لآخر الله لا تفعلن كذا أو قال والله لتفعلن كذا وقال الآخر ،نعم، إن أراد المبتدىء أن يحلف وأراد المجيب الحلف يكون كل منهما حالفاً لأن قوله نعم جواب والجواب يتضمن إعادة ما في السؤال فيصير كأنه قال نعم والله لأفعلن، وإن أراد المبتدي الاستحلاف وأراد المجيب الوعد ليس على كل واحد منهما شيء لأن كل واحد منهما نوى ما يحتمله وإن أراد المبتدي الاستحلاف وأراد المجيب الحلف فالمجيب الحالف والمبتدي لا لأن كل واحد منهما نوى ما يحتمله، وإن لم ينو واحد منهما شيئاً ففي قوله الله الحالف هو المجيب، وفي قوله والله الحالف هو المبتدي ا هـ . وأفاد بإطلاقه في اليمين بالله تعالى أنه لا يتوقف على النية ولا على العرف بل هو يمين تعارفوه أولاً . وهو الظاهر من مذهب أصحابنا وهو الصحيح كما في الذخيرة وغيرها إذ لا اعتبار بالعرف عند قيام دلالة النص ؛ كذا في المحيط . وبه اندفع ما في الولوالجية من أنه لو قال والرحمن لا أفعل كذا إن أراد به السورة لا يكون يميناً لأنه يصير كأنه قال والقرآن، وإن به الله تعالى يكون يميناً ا هـ . فإن هذا التفصيل في الرحمن قول بشر المريسي كما في الذخيرة والمذهب أنه يمين من غير نية. ومثل الحلف بالله الحلف بالذي لا إله إلا هو، ورب السموات والأرض ورب العالمين ومالك يوم الدين والأول الذي ليس قبله شيء، والآخر الذي ليس بعده شيء كما في فتح القدير. وأفاد بعطف الرحيم على الرحمن أنه لا أن أسمائه بین تكون خاصة أو مشتركة كالحكيم والعليم والقدير والعزيز، فالصحيح أنه لا يتوقف على النية خلافاً لبعض المشايخ فيما كان مشتركاً لأنه لما كان مستعملاً الله تعالى ولغيره لا تتعين إرادة أحدهما إلا بالنية. ورجحه في غاية البيان وهو خلاف المذهب لأن هذه الأسماء وإن كانت تطلق على الخلق لكن تعين الخالق مراداً بدلالة القسم إذ القسم بغير الله لا يجوز فكان الظاهر أنه أراد به اسم الله حملاً لكلامه على الصحة إلا أن ينوي به غير الله فلا يكون يميناً لأنه نوى ما يحتمله كلامه فيصدق في أمر بينه وبين الله تعالى؛ كذا في البدائع . وفي الذخيرة والولوالجية : لو قال والطالب والغالب لا أفعل كذا فهو يمين وهو متعارف أهل بغداد ا هـ. وهذا لا يدل على أن كونه يميناً موقوف على التعارف وإنما بعد ما حكم بكونها يميناً أخبر بأن أهل بغداد تعارفوا الحلف بها، وبذلك اندفع ما في فتح القدير من أنه يلزم إما اعتبار العرف فيما لم يسمع من الأسماء من الكتاب والسنة فإن الطالب لم يسمع بخصوصه بل قوله : ( وبذلك اندفع ما في فتح القدير الخ) قال المقدسي في شرحه أقول : أولاً الموجود في الولوالجية الطالب الغالب بغير واو ويدل على أن ذلك هو الصحيح قوله (يمين» ولو كان بواو لكان يمينين. وثانياً المحقق أراد إثبات كون اللفظ المذكور من أسمائه تعالى فلم يجد له دليلاً سوى الآية الدالة على كون غالباً صفة فجمعه مع الطالب جوز كونه يميناً كما أن الأول الذي ليس قبله شيء الغالب في قوله تعالى والله غالب على أمره [يوسف: ٢١] وإما كونه بناء على القول المفصل في الأسماء ا هـ .
Al-Walwalji mentioned: A man said to another, 'Allah, do not do such-and-such' or 'By Allah, do such-and-such,' and the other said, 'Yes.' If the initiator intended to swear and the respondent intended to swear, each of them is swearing because his saying 'yes' is a response, and the response includes repeating what is in the question, so it becomes as if he said, 'Yes, by Allah, I will do it.' If the initiator intended to make him swear and the respondent intended to promise, no oath is upon either of them because each intended what his words could bear. If the initiator intended to make him swear and the respondent intended to swear, then the respondent is the swearer, not the initiator, because each intended what his words could bear. If neither intended anything, then in the phrase 'Allah, do not do,' the swearer is the respondent; in the phrase 'By Allah, do not do,' the swearer is the initiator. End. His absolute statement about swearing by Allah Most High indicates that it does not depend on intention or custom; rather, it is an oath whether they are accustomed to it or not. This is the apparent position of our associates and it is the sound view as in al-Dhakhira etc., because custom is not considered when textual evidence exists; so in al-Muhit. By this is refuted what is in al-Walwaljiyya, that if one says 'By al-Rahman, I will not do such-and-such,' if he intended the Surah, it is not an oath because it becomes as if he said 'By the Quran'; but if he intended Allah, it is an oath. End. This detail about 'al-Rahman' is the saying of Bishr al-Marisi, as in al-Dhakhira; the school position is that it is an oath without intention. And swearing by Allah is like swearing by 'There is no god but Him', 'Lord of the heavens and earth', 'Lord of the worlds', 'Owner of the Day of Judgment', 'the First who was not before anything', 'the Last who is not after anything', as in Fath al-Qadir. And his coupling of al-Rahim with al-Rahman indicates that His names are either exclusive or shared, like al-Hakim, al-Alim, al-Qadir, al-Aziz. The correct view is that it does not depend on intention, contrary to some mashayikh regarding shared names, because since it is used for Allah and for others, one of them is not determined except by intention. He (al-Walwalji?) gave preponderance in Ghayat al-Bayan, but this is contrary to the madhhab, because these names, although they are applied to creation, the Creator being intended is determined by the context of the oath, since swearing by other than Allah is not permissible. So the apparent meaning is that he intended the name of Allah, interpreting his speech as sound, unless he intended other than Allah, then it is not an oath because he intended what his words could bear, and he is believed in matters between him and Allah; so in al-Bada'i. In al-Dhakhira and al-Walwaljiyya: If he said 'By al-Talib and al-Ghalib, I will not do such-and-such,' it is an oath, and it is the custom of the people of Baghdad. End. This does not indicate that it being an oath depends on custom; rather, after ruling that it is an oath, he informed that the people of Baghdad customarily swore by it. By this is refuted what is in Fath al-Qadir, that it would require either considering custom regarding names not heard in the Book and Sunna, for 'al-Talib' is not specifically heard. Rather, his statement (and by this is refuted what is in Fath al-Qadir etc.) al-Maqdisi said in his commentary: I say: Firstly, what is found in al-Walwaljiyya is 'al-Talib al-Ghalib' without waw, and that indicates that this is correct, because his saying 'yamin' (oath) [shows]; if it were with waw, it would be two oaths. Secondly, the muhaqqiq intended to prove that the mentioned word is among His names, so he found no evidence except the verse indicating that 'Ghalib' is an attribute, so he combined it with al-Talib to permit it being an oath, just as 'the First who was not before anything' and 'al-Ghalib' in His statement 'And Allah is Ghalib over His affair' [Yusuf 12:21], or it being based on the detailed view about names. End.
Source pages p. 474

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings about oaths, focusing on how a response to an oath can itself become an oath based on the intention of the parties. The author first describes a situation where one person says to another 'Allah, do not do such-and-such' or 'By Allah, do such-and-such' and the other responds 'Yes.' The ruling depends on the intentions of both. If both intended to swear, then each is a swearer; the response 'yes' is considered a repetition of the oath phrase. If the initiator intended to make the other swear and the respondent intended only to promise, no oath occurs. If the initiator intended to make the other swear and the respondent intended to swear, only the respondent is bound. If neither intended anything, the identity of the swearer depends on the wording used: 'Allah' makes the respondent the swearer, 'Wallah' makes the initiator the swearer. The passage then expands on the principle that swearing by Allah's names—like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, al-Hakim, etc.—is an oath regardless of intention or custom, contrary to the view of Bishr al-Marisi who required intention for al-Rahman. The Hanafi school holds that even shared names like al-Hakim (used for both Allah and wise people) do not require intention for the oath to be valid, because the context of swearing indicates the Creator, unless the swearer explicitly intended otherwise. The passage also discusses the names 'al-Talib' and 'al-Ghalib,' which are not found in the Quran or Sunna as names of Allah, but the custom of the people of Baghdad made them valid oaths. The author refutes an objection from Fath al-Qadir by showing that the ruling does not depend on custom alone but on the interpretation of the word 'al-Ghalib' as an attribute from the Quran. This lesson thus clarifies the importance of intention in oath responses and the permissibility of swearing by recognized divine names and attributes, even if not mentioned in the revealed texts.

Key Terms
الاستحلاف (al-istihlaf): The act of making another person swear an oath (i.e., demanding an oath from someone).
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or His attributes, creating a legal obligation to fulfill or expiate.
الجواب (al-jawab): The response to an oath statement, which can be considered a repetition of the oath wording if intention aligns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the initiator says 'Allah, do not do that' and the respondent says 'Na'am' intending to swear, but the initiator intended only to make the respondent swear (istihlaf), what is the ruling?
A) Both are bound by an oath.
B) Only the initiator is bound.
C) Only the respondent is bound.
D) Neither is bound.

Key Takeaway
The response 'na'am' to an oath becomes a binding oath only when the respondent intends to swear; otherwise, no oath is formed.

Swearing by Allah's Names: The Hanafi Position That Intention and Custom Are Not Required
Explanation that swearing by Allah's names is an oath without need for intention or custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that swearing by any of Allah's names is a valid oath regardless of intention or custom, and evaluate the opposing view of Bishr al-Marisi.

Core Idea
Swearing by Allah's names is an oath without need for intention or custom – the name alone suffices.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on oaths made by invoking Allah's names. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq asserts that swearing by Allah's names, whether exclusive (like Allah) or shared (like al-Hakim), constitutes a binding oath without requiring the swearer's intention or customary usage. This is a robust position grounded in the textual evidence that an oath by Allah is sacred. We will also explore the minority view of Bishr al-Marisi, who required intention for certain names, and see how the Hanafi school refutes it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scope of 'Swearing by Allah'
Note that the passage includes not just the name 'Allah' but also other names like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, and even shared names like al-Hakim, al-Alim. The ruling applies to all of them.
The list also includes descriptive phrases like 'Lord of the worlds' and 'Owner of the Day of Judgment'.
2
Understand the Two Opposing Views on Al-Rahman
Bishr al-Marisi held that swearing by al-Rahman requires intention because it could refer to the Surah. The Hanafi school, however, says it is an oath without intention.
The Hanafi argument: the context of swearing (qasam) is only valid if directed to Allah, so the default is that the swearer means Allah.
3
Analyze the Ruling on Shared Names
Shared names like al-Hakim (wise) are used for both Allah and humans. Some later scholars required intention for these, but the relied-upon Hanafi position is that they, too, are oaths without intention.
The reasoning: the act of swearing (qasam) indicates the Creator is intended, unless the swearer explicitly states otherwise.
4
Examine the Role of Custom in Unfamiliar Names
The passage discusses names like 'al-Talib' and 'al-Ghalib,' which are not explicitly in the Quran or Sunna. The Hanafi ruling is that they are oaths based on customary usage (ta'aruf) among the people of Baghdad.
This shows that custom can establish a name as a valid oath subject, but the ruling itself does not depend on custom; the custom only confirms usage.
5
Extract the Principle: No Need for Intention or Custom
The overarching principle is that swearing by Allah's names is a valid oath by default. Intention is only relevant if the swearer explicitly intends something other than Allah (like a created entity).
This is the mufta bihi position of the Hanafi school.

Source Text

Al-Walwalji mentioned: A man said to another, 'Allah, do not do such-and-such' or 'By Allah, do such-and-such,' and the other said, 'Yes.' If the initiator intended to swear and the respondent intended to swear, each of them is swearing because his saying 'yes' is a response, and the response includes repeating what is in the question, so it becomes as if he said, 'Yes, by Allah, I will do it.' If the initiator intended to make him swear and the respondent intended to promise, no oath is upon either of them because each intended what his words could bear. If the initiator intended to make him swear and the respondent intended to swear, then the respondent is the swearer, not the initiator, because each intended what his words could bear. If neither intended anything, then in the phrase 'Allah, do not do,' the swearer is the respondent; in the phrase 'By Allah, do not do,' the swearer is the initiator. End. End.
