Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings about oaths, focusing on how a response to an oath can itself become an oath based on the intention of the parties. The author first describes a situation where one person says to another 'Allah, do not do such-and-such' or 'By Allah, do such-and-such' and the other responds 'Yes.' The ruling depends on the intentions of both. If both intended to swear, then each is a swearer; the response 'yes' is considered a repetition of the oath phrase. If the initiator intended to make the other swear and the respondent intended only to promise, no oath occurs. If the initiator intended to make the other swear and the respondent intended to swear, only the respondent is bound. If neither intended anything, the identity of the swearer depends on the wording used: 'Allah' makes the respondent the swearer, 'Wallah' makes the initiator the swearer. The passage then expands on the principle that swearing by Allah's names—like al-Rahman, al-Rahim, al-Hakim, etc.—is an oath regardless of intention or custom, contrary to the view of Bishr al-Marisi who required intention for al-Rahman. The Hanafi school holds that even shared names like al-Hakim (used for both Allah and wise people) do not require intention for the oath to be valid, because the context of swearing indicates the Creator, unless the swearer explicitly intended otherwise. The passage also discusses the names 'al-Talib' and 'al-Ghalib,' which are not found in the Quran or Sunna as names of Allah, but the custom of the people of Baghdad made them valid oaths. The author refutes an objection from Fath al-Qadir by showing that the ruling does not depend on custom alone but on the interpretation of the word 'al-Ghalib' as an attribute from the Quran. This lesson thus clarifies the importance of intention in oath responses and the permissibility of swearing by recognized divine names and attributes, even if not mentioned in the revealed texts.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or His attributes, creating a legal obligation to fulfill or expiate.
الرحمن (al-Rahman): One of Allah's names; also the name of a surah. The Hanafi school holds that swearing by it is an oath without intention.
القسم (al-qasam): The act of swearing; the context of an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, is swearing by 'al-Hakim' (the Wise) a valid oath without intention, given that 'al-Hakim' can also describe a wise person?
A) Yes, because the context of swearing indicates Allah is intended.
B) No, because the name is shared and requires intention to specify.
C) Yes, but only if the local community customarily uses it in oaths.
D) No, because 'al-Hakim' is not a name of Allah in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by any of Allah's names is a valid oath regardless of intention or custom, because the act of swearing itself indicates that Allah is intended.

Swearing by Divine Attributes: Custom and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on validity of oaths by divine attributes, with focus on custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths by Allah's attributes, distinguish the Iraqi and general views, and apply the custom-based criterion to determine oath validity.

Core Idea
An oath by Allah's attributes is valid only if customarily used, regardless of whether the attribute is of essence or action.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school regarding oaths sworn by Allah's attributes. Early Iraqi scholars distinguished between attributes of essence (always valid) and action (invalid), while later Hanafis held that all attributes are eternal and equally valid, but only if people customarily swear by them. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how custom ('urf) functions as a source of law in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
Determine the subject: swearing by an attribute of Allah (e.g., 'By the mercy of Allah'). Recognize that the central issue is whether such an oath is legally binding (a valid yamin).
The dispute is within the Hanafi school, not with other madhahib, although theological sects like Karamiyya and Mu'tazila are mentioned.
2
State the Iraqi Position and Its Distinction
The Iraqi mashayikh held that only attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) form valid oaths (except 'ilm), while attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l) do not. Understand their criterion: attributes describable by opposites (e.g., mercy vs. anger) are of action; those without opposites (e.g., power) are of essence.
'Ilm (knowledge) is excluded despite being an attribute of essence, for a reason stated elsewhere.
3
State the General Hanafi Position Requiring Custom
The generality of Hanafi mashayikh (as in al-Muhit) held that all attributes are equally sacred and eternal; the sole criterion is custom ('urf). If people customarily swear by an attribute, it is an oath; otherwise not.
This view aligns with the later Hanafi doctrine that attributes of action are also eternal (from Abu Mansur).
4
Analyze the Later Hanafi Refinement
Later Hanafis (from Abu Mansur) argued that attributes of action are eternal, so they are as valid as attributes of essence. Thus, custom becomes the decisive factor, not the type of attribute. This is supported by Fath al-Qadir and al-Tabyin.
This refinement resolves the inconsistency in the Iraqi position and aligns with the general reliance on custom in oaths.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Its Basis
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that swearing by any attribute of Allah is valid if customarily used. This view is preferred because it is simpler, avoids distinguishing between types of attributes, and is supported by later authoritative texts like Fath al-Qadir and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
The mufta bihi is based on fatawa mutakhkhirin (later fatwa works) and the principle that oaths are built on 'urf.

من صفات الذات يكون يميناً إلا العلم لما تبين وإن حلف بصفة من صفات الفعل لا يكون يميناً، والفاصل بينهما إن كل صفة يوصف بها وبضدها كالرحمة والرأفة والسخط والغضب فهي من صفات الفعل، كل صفة يوصف بها ولا يوصف بضدها كالقدرة والعزة والعظمة فهي من صفات الذات، فألحقوا صفات الذات بالاسم ولم يلحقوا صفات الفعل بالاسم وعلى هذا تخرج المسائل ا هـ . لا وظاهره أن الكرامية مؤمنون والمعتزلة كافرون لدعائه للأولين بالهداية وعلى المعتزلة باللعن. وفي فتح القدير : المراد بالصفة اسم المعنى الذي لا يتضمن ذاتاً ولا يحمل عليها بهو هو كالعزة والكبرياء والعظمة بخلاف نحو العظيم. وفي التبيين والصحيح عدم الفرق لأن صفات الله كلها صفات ذات وكلها قديمة فلا يستقيم الفرق والأيمان مبنية على العرف فما تعارف الناس الحلف به يكون يميناً وما لا فلا ا هـ . وفي المسايرة للمحقق ابن الهمام: اختلف مشايخ الحنفية والأشاعرة في صفات الأفعال والمراد صفات تدل على تأثير لها أسماء غير اسم القدرة يجمعها اسم التكوين فإن كان ذلك الأثر مخلوقاً فالاسم الخالق والصفة الخلق، أو رزقاً فالاسم الرازق والصفة الترزيق، أو حياة فهو المحيي، أو موتاً فهو المميت. فادعى متأخروا الحنيفة من عهد أبي منصور أنها صفات قديمة زائدة على الصفات المتقدمة صار بالوصف مختصاً به تعالى فساغ الحلف به فهذا يدل على أن ذكرهم التعارف به هو الذي سوغ كونه يميناً أو أيده فكيف يندفع كلام الكمال بما فيه احتمال ولا تصريح بما يخالفه اهـ. قلت: ويؤيده ما في مختارات النوازل حيث قال وقوله الطالب الغالب لا أفعل كذا فهو يمين لتعارف أهل بغداد اهـ. فهذا لا يحتمل التأويل الذي ذكره المؤلف أصلاً قوله : (ولم يقيد المصنف الحلف بالصفات بالعرف) قال في النهر أقول : ممنوع فقد أشار إلى ذلك بقوله «لا بعلمه الخ».
And his statement 'and His Majesty and His Greatness' indicates that swearing can be by an attribute of His attributes, because the meaning of oath—which is strength—is achieved since he believes in the veneration of Allah and His attributes. The author did not restrict swearing by attributes to custom, but it is necessary. Al-Muhit says: Regarding swearing by Allah's attributes, the statements of our mashayikh differ. The generality of our mashayikh said: Whoever swears by an attribute of Allah's attributes, whether an attribute of essence (dhat) or an attribute of action (fi'l), it is examined. If people customarily swear by it, it is an oath; otherwise not, because Allah's attributes in sanctity are like His essence—they are not other than Allah; rather, they are attributes—neither He nor other—because they are not originated in His essence, contrary to what the Karamiyya say (may Allah guide them) that Allah has originated attributes and His essence is the locus of originated things, and contrary to what the Mu'tazila say (may Allah curse them) that He has no attributes. According to Ahl al-Sunna (may Allah multiply them), His attributes of essence are that He is Hearing, Seeing, Living, Knowing, Powerful; He is eternal with all His attributes, and the Eternal cannot be the locus of originated things. The 'Iraqi mashayikh said: If he swears by an attribute of essence, it is an oath, except for 'knowledge' for a reason; if he swears by an attribute of action, it is not an oath. The distinction between them is that every attribute which is described and can be described by its opposite, like mercy, compassion, displeasure, anger—these are attributes of action; every attribute which is described and not described by its opposite, like power, might, greatness—these are attributes of essence. So they annexed attributes of essence to the name, and did not annex attributes of action to the name; on this are based the issues. End. Its apparent meaning is that the Karamiyya are believers and the Mu'tazila are disbelievers, because he invoked guidance for the former and curse on the latter. In Fath al-Qadir: The meaning of 'attribute' is the name of a meaning that does not entail a substance and is not predicated by 'huwa huwa', like might, greatness, and majesty, unlike 'the Mighty', etc. In al-Tabyin, the sound view is no distinction, because all of Allah's attributes are attributes of essence and all are eternal, so the distinction is not sound; oaths are based on custom, so whatever people customarily swear by is an oath, otherwise not. End. In al-Musayara of Ibn al-Humam: The Hanafi and Ash'ari mashayikh differed about attributes of actions; what is meant are attributes indicating an effect; they have names other than the name 'power' gathered by the name 'takwin'. If that effect is created, the name is 'the Creator' and the attribute is 'creating'; if provision, the name 'the Provider' and attribute 'providing'; if life, He is 'the Life-Giver'; if death, 'the Death-Giver'. The later Hanafis from the time of Abu Mansur claimed that these are eternal attributes additional to the preceding attributes, so swearing by them is permissible because the attribute specifies Him. This indicates that their mention of custom is what permitted it being an oath or supported it; so how can the words of al-Kamal be refuted by what contains possibility but no explicit contrary? End. I say: This is supported by what is in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil, where he said: 'And the statement al-Talib al-Ghalib I will not do such-and-such is an oath due to the custom of the people of Baghdad.' This cannot bear the interpretation mentioned by the author originally. His statement: 'And the author did not restrict swearing by attributes to custom.' In al-Nahr he said: I say: This is not permissible, for he indicated that by his statement 'except by His knowledge etc.'
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into the issue of swearing by the attributes of Allah, an area where Hanafi scholars differed. The passage opens by noting that the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq (the matn) did not explicitly restrict swearing by attributes to custom, but the commentator (Ibn Nujaym) asserts that custom is necessary. He then cites from al-Muhit the general Hanafi view that swearing by any attribute of Allah—whether of essence (dhat) or action (fi'l)—requires customary usage; if people customarily swear by it, it is an oath, otherwise not. This is because divine attributes are as sacred as the essence itself, and they are neither identical to nor separate from Allah. The passage then contrasts this with the Iraqi scholars' view, who distinguished between attributes of essence (e.g., power, might) which always form valid oaths (except knowledge), and attributes of action (e.g., mercy, anger) which do not. The distinction is based on whether the attribute can be described with its opposite: attributes like power cannot be opposed (no 'weakness' in Allah), whereas mercy has a counterpart (anger). The commentator also notes the theological overtones, calling the Karamiyya (who held attributes are originated) believers worthy of guidance, and the Mu'tazila (who denied attributes) disbelievers worthy of curse. Later, from Fath al-Qadir, a simpler view is presented: all attributes are essentially the same—eternal—so the only criterion is custom. The dispute among later Hanafis and Ash'aris about attributes of action (like 'creator') is also examined; the later Hanafis held these attributes are also eternal, making oaths by them valid, again supported by customary usage. The passage concludes by reinforcing that the ruling in al-Walwaljiyya about 'al-Talib al-Ghalib' was based on custom, not on textual authority. Thus, this lesson highlights the tension between the need for custom in oaths by attributes and the theological stance that all attributes are equally eternal and sacred.

Key Terms
صفة الذات (sifat al-dhat): Attributes of Allah's essence that are inseparable from Him, like power, knowledge, life. These are eternal and have no opposite.
صفة الفعل (sifat al-fi'l): Attributes of Allah that relate to His actions, like creating, providing, giving life. These have opposites (e.g., mercy vs. anger).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the mufta bihi position on swearing by attributes of Allah?
A) Swearing by attributes of essence is valid; attributes of action are not.
B) Swearing by any attribute is valid only if customarily used.
C) Swearing by attributes is invalid because attributes are not names of Allah.
D) Swearing by attributes is valid only if the attribute is one of the names of Allah.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that oaths by Allah's attributes are valid only if customarily used, rejecting any distinction between attributes of essence and action.

Validity of Oaths by Divine Attributes: Custom vs. Sifat Distinction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oaths by divine attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on oaths by divine attributes and apply the distinguishing criterion between sifat al-dhat and sifat al-fi'l.

Core Idea
An oath by a divine attribute is valid only if customary or if the attribute is a property of essence (sifat al-dhat).

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an internal Hanafi disagreement on whether swearing by Allah's attributes constitutes a valid oath. The general mashayikh rely on custom, while the Iraqi mashayikh distinguish between attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) and attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l). Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for correctly determining the binding nature of such oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
The passage discusses whether an oath (yamin) sworn by a divine attribute of Allah is valid and binding. Two Hanafi views are presented.
2
State the first position (general mashayikh)
The majority view: validity depends on customary usage ('urf). If people customarily swear by that attribute, it is an oath; otherwise not.
This view is based on the sanctity of attributes being like the essence.
3
State the second position (Iraqi mashayikh)
The Iraqi view distinguishes between attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) and attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l). Oaths by the former are valid (except knowledge), by the latter invalid.
The criterion: if an attribute has an opposite (e.g., mercy/wrath), it is action; if no opposite (e.g., power), it is essence.
4
Analyze the theological background
The passage refutes deviant theologies (Karamiyya, Mu'tazila) and affirms the Sunni position that attributes are eternal and not created.
This underpins the sanctity of attributes.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the general view, any customarily used attribute forms a valid oath. Under the Iraqi view, only attributes without opposites (except knowledge) qualify, limiting the scope of valid oaths.

Book of Oaths. The predominant in His statement, 'And Allah is predominant over His affair' [Yusuf 12:21] and it being based on the detailed view regarding names, end. And He informed by His saying 'His Majesty and His Greatness' that the oath is by an attribute of His attributes because the meaning of yamin (oath) which is strength is obtained because he believes in the glorification of Allah and His attributes. And the author did not restrict swearing by attributes to custom, but it is necessary. He said in al-Muhit: 'As for swearing by attributes of Allah, the expressions of our mashayikh have differed in that. The generality of our mashayikh said: whoever swears by an attribute of Allah, whether an attribute of essence or an attribute of action, it is considered; if people are accustomed to swearing by it, it is an oath, otherwise not, because the attributes of Allah in sanctity are like His essence; they are not other than Allah, but attributes of Allah are neither He nor other than Him, because they are not originated in His essence, contrary to what the Karamiyya say (may Allah guide them) that Allah has originated attributes and His essence is the locus of originated things, and contrary to what the Mu'tazila say (may Allah curse them) that Allah has no attributes. And according to Ahl al-Sunnah (may Allah multiply them), His attribute of essence is His being hearing, seeing, living, knowing, powerful, and He with all His attributes is eternal, and the eternal may be the locus of originated things. And the mashayikh of Iraq said: if he swears by an attribute of essence, it is an oath except knowledge as will appear, and if he swears by an attribute of action, it is not an oath. And the differentiator between them is: every attribute which He is described with and its opposite is also described (like mercy, compassion, wrath, anger) are attributes of action; every attribute which He is described with but not described with its opposite (like power, might, greatness) are attributes of essence. So they attached attributes of essence to the name and did not attach attributes of action to the name. And upon this issues are based, end.'

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion probe on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding oaths by divine attributes. The author begins by commenting on the phrase 'al-ghalib' in the Quranic verse, linking it to the detailed view on names. The main issue is whether an oath by a divine attribute constitutes a valid yamin. The author notes that the author of Kanz did not restrict the ruling to customary usage, but this is essential. He then quotes al-Muhit, which reports a difference among Hanafi mashayikh. The majority view (عامة مشايخنا) is that it depends on custom: if people customarily swear by that attribute, it is a valid oath; otherwise not. This is because attributes are like the essence in sanctity; they are not created. Two theological deviant groups are mentioned: the Karamiyya (who say attributes are created) and the Mu'tazila (who deny attributes). The correct Sunni position is that attributes are eternal. Then there is a specific view of the Iraqi mashayikh: they distinguish between attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) and attributes of action (sifat al-fi'l). Oaths by the former are valid (except knowledge), but oaths by the latter are not. The criterion: if an attribute has an opposite (e.g., mercy vs. wrath), it is an attribute of action; if it has no opposite (e.g., power), it is an attribute of essence. They analogized attributes of essence to the name, but not attributes of action. The passage concludes that rulings are based on this distinction.

Key Terms
صفات الذات (ṣifāt al-dhāt): Attributes that are essential to Allah and have no opposite, e.g., power, might, greatness.
صفات الفعل (ṣifāt al-fiʿl): Attributes that describe Allah's actions and have opposites, e.g., mercy, wrath.
اليمين (al-yamīn): An oath that invokes Allah or His attributes, binding when properly uttered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Power (qudra)
B) Might (ʿizza)
C) Wrath (ghadab)
D) Greatness (ʿaẓama)

Key Takeaway
The validity of an oath by a divine attribute in the Hanafi school depends either on customary usage or on the attribute being from sifat al-dhat (without opposite), with knowledge being an exception.

Custom as the Decisive Criterion for Oaths by Attributes
Critique of the distinction between attributes and affirmation of custom as sole criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that oaths are built on custom to determine the validity of an oath by a divine attribute, and explain why the later Hanafi position rejects a theological distinction between attributes of essence and action.

Core Idea
Oaths are built on custom, not on theological distinctions; custom alone determines validity.

This passage dives into a nuanced debate: does the validity of swearing by a divine attribute depend on whether that attribute is eternal or not? Some early Hanafis entertained a distinction between attributes of essence and action, but later authorities decisively settled that such theological classifications are irrelevant. The key principle is simple and practical: oaths follow custom (urf). Whatever people customarily use to swear becomes a binding oath, regardless of its category in kalam. This lesson unpacks that shift and its implications for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
The passage contrasts two views: one that differentiates between attributes of essence and action, and the later Hanafi view that all attributes are eternal and custom alone matters.
The older view attempted to restrict valid oaths to attributes of essence.
2
Locate the custom-based ruling
Al-Tabyin states: 'al-aymān mabniyya ʿalā al-ʿurf' (oaths are built on custom). This is the decisive principle.
Custom overrides any theological classification.
3
Examine the theological background
Later Hanafis (from Abu Mansur) asserted that attributes of action (e.g., creating, providing) are eternal, additional attributes. This supports the ruling that swearing by them is valid.
This aligns the fiqh ruling with a specific kalam position, but custom remains the primary criterion.
4
Note the Ash'ari objection
Ash'aris reduce takwin to power with a specific relation, denying a separate eternal attribute. The passage implies that even so, custom would still validate the oath in practice.
The fatwa relies on custom, so the theological ikhtilaf often has no practical effect.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Consider an oath by 'al-Khāliq' (the Creator). In a community where this is customary, it is a valid oath. If not customary, it is not a binding oath.
The burden is on the mufti to ascertain local custom.

وظاهره أن الكرامية مؤمنون والمعتزلة كافرون لدعائه للأولين بالهداية وعلى المعتزلة باللعن. وفي فتح القدير : المراد بالصفة اسم المعنى الذي لا يتضمن ذاتاً ولا يحمل عليها بهو هو كالعزة والكبرياء والعظمة بخلاف نحو العظيم. وفي التبيين والصحيح عدم الفرق لأن صفات الله كلها صفات ذات وكلها قديمة فلا يستقيم الفرق والأيمان مبنية على العرف فما تعارف الناس الحلف به يكون يميناً وما لا فلا ا هـ . وفي المسايرة للمحقق ابن الهمام: اختلف مشايخ الحنفية والأشاعرة في صفات الأفعال والمراد صفات تدل على تأثير لها أسماء غير اسم القدرة يجمعها اسم التكوين فإن كان ذلك الأثر مخلوقاً فالاسم الخالق والصفة الخلق، أو رزقاً فالاسم الرازق والصفة الترزيق، أو حياة فهو المحيي، أو موتاً فهو المميت. فادعى متأخروا الحنيفة من عهد أبي منصور أنها صفات قديمة زائدة على الصفات المتقدمة صار بالوصف مختصاً به تعالى فساغ الحلف به فهذا يدل على أن ذكرهم التعارف به هو الذي سوغ كونه يميناً أو أيده فكيف يندفع كلام الكمال بما فيه احتمال ولا تصريح بما يخالفه اهـ. قلت: ويؤيده ما في مختارات النوازل حيث قال وقوله الطالب الغالب لا أفعل كذا فهو يمين لتعارف أهل بغداد اهـ. فهذا لا يحتمل التأويل الذي ذكره المؤلف أصلاً قوله : (ولم يقيد المصنف الحلف بالصفات بالعرف) قال في النهر أقول : ممنوع فقد أشار إلى ذلك بقوله «لا بعلمه الخ».
وليس في كلام أبي حنيفة والمتقدمين تصريح بذلك سوى ما أخذوه من قوله كان تعالى خالقاً قبل أن يخلق ورازقاً قبل أن يرزق وذكروا له أوجهاً من الاستدلال والأشاعرة يقولون ليست صفة التكوين على فصولها سوى صفة القدرة باعتبار تعلقها بتعلق خاص، فالتخليق هو القدرة باعتبار تعلقها بالمخلوق والترزيق تعلقها بإيصال الرزق إلى آخر ما ذكره فيها.
And its apparent meaning is that the Karamiyya are believers and the Mu'tazila are disbelievers, because he supplicates for guidance for the former and curses the latter. And in Fath al-Qadir: 'By attribute is meant the name of a meaning that does not include an essence and is not predicated of it with 'huwa huwa', like might, majesty, greatness, contrary to something like al-ʿAẓīm.' And in al-Tabyin: 'The correct view is no distinction, because all of Allah's attributes are attributes of essence and all are eternal, so the distinction is not sound. And oaths are built on custom; whatever people customarily swear by is an oath, and whatever not, then not.' And in al-Musayara by the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humann: 'The mashayikh of the Hanafiyya and the Ashʿariyya differed regarding attributes of actions. What is meant are attributes that indicate an effect; they have names other than the name of power, encompassed by the name takwīn (origination). So if that effect is created, then the name is al-Khāliq and the attribute is khalq; or provision, then al-Rāziq and tarzīq; or life, then al-Muḥyī; or death, then al-Mumīt. The later Hanafis from the time of Abū Manṣūr claimed they are eternal attributes additional to the preceding attributes; He is characterized by them exclusively, so swearing by them is permissible. This indicates that their mention of custom is what allowed it to be an oath or supported it. So how can the statement of al-Kamāl be refuted by something that contains probability and no explicit statement opposing it?' He said: 'And it is supported by what is in Mukhtārāt al-Nawāzil where he said: 'And his saying, al-ṭālib al-ghālib 'I will not do such and such' is an oath due to the custom of the people of Baghdad.' So this does not admit the interpretation mentioned by the author originally. His saying: 'And the author did not restrict swearing by attributes to custom.' He said in al-Nahr: 'I say: it is denied, for he indicated that by his saying 'not by His knowledge etc.'
And there is no explicit statement in the speech of Abū Ḥanīfa and the early scholars regarding that, except what they derived from His saying: 'He, may He be exalted, was Creator before He created, and Provider before He provided,' and they mentioned ways of deduction for it. And the Ashʿariyya say: 'The attribute of takwīn according to its divisions is nothing other than the attribute of power with consideration of its specific attachment. So takhlīq (making) is power with consideration of its attachment to the created, and tarzīq is its attachment to delivering provision,' to the end of what he mentioned therein.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This critical refinement passage continues the discussion on oaths by divine attributes. It opens by noting that the previous text implies the Karamiyya are believers (since prayer for guidance) and the Mu'tazila disbelievers (since cursing). Then it cites Fath al-Qadir's definition of 'attribute', followed by al-Tabyin's statement that there is no valid distinction between attributes of essence and action; all are eternal and oaths depend on custom, not on attribute type. Then al-Musayara of Ibn al-Humann presents the dispute between Hanafi and Ashʿari mashayikh regarding attributes of action (ṣifāt al-afʿāl). The later Hanafis (from Abu Mansur's time) hold these attributes (like creating, providing) as eternal, additional to the essential attributes, so swearing by them is valid. This supports the custom criterion. The author quotes Mukhtārāt al-Nawāzil: the phrase 'al-ṭālib al-ghālib' is an oath due to Baghdadi custom. Then a comment on the author of Kanz: he is criticised for not restricting to custom, but al-Nahr defends that he did indirectly. Finally, the passage notes that Abu Hanifa and early scholars had no explicit statement; they deduced from the Qur'anic phrasing 'He was Creator before creating'. The Ashʿaris reduce the attribute of takwīn to power with a specific relation, denying it as a separate eternal attribute. This entire discussion reinforces that the Hanafi position relies on custom (urf) for the validity of oaths by attributes, not on speculative distinctions.

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال (ṣifāt al-afʿāl): Attributes of action; attributes that describe Allah's creative acts, e.g., creating, providing.
التكوين (al-takwīn): The attribute of origination; that by which Allah brings things into existence.
العرف (al-ʿurf): Customary usage of a locality; determines whether an oath by a phrase is legally binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage from al-Tabyin, what is the correct basis for determining if an oath by a divine attribute is valid?
A) Whether the attribute is of essence or action
B) Whether the attribute is mentioned in the Quran
C) Whether people customarily swear by that attribute
D) Whether the attribute has an opposite

Key Takeaway
The validity of an oath by a divine name depends on local custom, not on whether the attribute is considered eternal or relational in kalam.

Oath Formulas: When Do 'Aqsam', 'Ahilfu', 'Ashhad', and 'Ala Yamin Bind?
Ruling on specific phrases as oaths, with proof from Quran and intra-Hanafi analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an oath using 'aqsam', 'ahilfu', or 'ashhad' requires mention of the thing sworn upon to be binding, and distinguish the exceptional treatment of 'ala yamin' as a vow formula.

Core Idea
No binding oath without mentioning the act sworn upon—unless you say 'ala yamin', which is a vow.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq investigates whether certain Arabic expressions automatically create a binding oath (yamīn munʿaqidah) simply by being uttered, or whether further conditions are needed. The author examines the formulas 'aqsam', 'ahilfu', 'ashhad', and 'ala yamin', citing Quranic proof and engaging with intra-Hanafi differences. Understanding this ruling is critical for muftis and judges who must determine when an oath is concluded and when kaffarah becomes obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
The passage begins by stating that saying 'aqsam', 'ahilfu', or 'ashhad' even without 'billah' can be an oath because these words are customarily used for swearing. The Quranic verse (al-Munafiqun 63:1-2) is cited to prove that 'ashhad' is treated as an oath.
The dalīl connects 'ashhad' to 'aymān' (oaths) in the Quran.
2
Recognize the condition for a binding oath
The author clarifies that these words only become a concluded oath if the person mentions the thing sworn upon (al-muqsam ʿalayhi). Without that specification, no oath is formed and no kaffarah is due upon breaking.
Imam Muhammad's al-Asl lists these as oaths but with this condition.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on 'ashhad' and 'ala yamin'
Al-Mujtaba states that 'ashhad' is not an oath unless attached to a condition. For 'ala yamin', some early works (al-Nihaya, al-Dhakhira) considered it a binding oath even without mention of the act, but this is rejected by most later authorities. However, Fath al-Qadir treats 'ala yamin' as a vow formula that obligates kaffarah directly.
This shows the range of opinion within the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the reasoning for requiring the thing sworn upon
The passage explains that without specifying the act, there is no basis for concluding an oath, and hence no breaking (ḥinth) that triggers kaffarah. Additionally, 'ala yamin' is ambiguous (could be ghamus or munʿaqidah), so kaffarah cannot be imposed by probability.
Legal maxim: punishments and obligations are not established by doubt.
5
Distinguish the exceptional treatment of 'ala yamin'
Fath al-Qadir treats 'ala yamin' as an inceptive expression akin to a vow (nadhr), not as an oath. Therefore, it creates an initial obligation of kaffarah even without a specific act, unlike 'half' and 'ashhad' which are not vow formulas.
This is the muftā bihī position according to this source.

المنعقدة فعلى أي شيء انعقدت اليمين حتى يتصور نقض اليمين فتجب الكفارة. وأيضاً قوله على يمين فيه احتمال لأنه عليه أن يكون يصح يمين الغموس أو اليمين المنعقدة والكفارة لا تثبت بالاحتمال لأنها دائرة بين And as for him contradicting by saying 'I swear' (uqsim) or 'I take an oath' (ahilfu) or 'I bear witness' (ashhad) even if he does not say 'by Allah', it is because these words are used in swearing, and this form is literally for the present tense and is used for the future with an indication, so he is made a swearer for the present tense, and bearing witness is an oath. Allah said: 'They said, we bear witness that you are the Messenger of Allah' [al-Munafiqun: 1], then He said: 'They have taken their oaths as a shield' [al-Munafiqun: 2]. And swearing by Allah is the established, legislated way; swearing by other than Him is prohibited, so it is turned to it. And he indicated that if he says 'I swore' (haliftu) or 'I took an oath' (aqsamtu) or 'I bore witness' (shahidtu) with 'by Allah' or without, it is an oath a fortiori. And he spoke absolutely regarding it being an oath with the present tense verb, so it shows that it does not depend on intention, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And there is a difference mentioned in al-Hidayah regarding it. And in al-Tabyin it is corrected that it is an oath without intention. And the author meant by these words that each of them is suitable to be an oath; if he mentions the thing sworn upon, the oath is concluded, so he breaks it and becomes liable for kaffarah; otherwise not. And Muhammad mentioned all these words in al-Asl, then said after them: 'These are all oaths; if one swears by any of them to do such-and-such and breaks it, kaffarah becomes obligatory.' And in al-Mujtaba: 'Ashhad is not an oath unless he attaches it to a condition; and his saying 'upon me a vow' is an oath even if silent.' And in al-Muntaqa and Jami' al-Karkhi there is something resembling a disagreement regarding the issue of nadhr. I say: by this it is known that these words are not an oath unless attached to something. So it appears that what is in al-Nihaya, that his saying 'I swear' (uqsim) or 'I bear witness' (ashhad) or 'upon me an oath' (ala yamin) constitutes a binding oath whether he mentions the thing sworn upon or not, based on what is mentioned in al-Dhakhira that his saying 'ala yamin' necessitates kaffarah, is an oversight as in Ghayat al-Bayan, and a delusion and confusion as in Fath al-Qadir. Rather, it is necessary to mention the thing sworn upon; and its omission in some places is due to the context making it known, and that is the intention of the author of al-Dhakhira. The verification is that kaffarah is only obligatory to cover the sin in breaking a concluded oath; so on what basis was the oath concluded so that breaking it is possible and kaffarah obligatory? Also, his saying 'ala yamin' contains probability: it could be a valid oath (yamin ghamus) or a concluded oath; and kaffarah is not established by probability because it is between worship and punishment, and punishments are repelled by doubts. Since there is no kaffarah for ghamus, nor for a concluded oath when evidence of fulfillment arises, how can kaffarah be imagined? Also, if kaffarah were obligatory merely by saying 'ala yamin', it would entail the consequence preceding the cause, which is invalid, because the cause of kaffarah is the breaking (hanth), which has not occurred due to lack of an oath on anything. To the end of what is in Ghayat al-Bayan. However, in Fath al-Qadir it is said: 'The truth is that his saying 'ala yamin' if he does not add anything, as an inceptive (insha) not a report (ikhbar), obligates kaffarah based on the fact that it is an undertaking of kaffarah with this expression from the beginning, as will come in his saying 'ala nadhr' (upon me a vow) if he does not add anything; indeed it is similar to the formulas of nadhr. And if it were not so, it would be void, unlike 'half' and 'ashhad' and the like, which are not formulas of nadhr, so an undertaking is not established from the beginning, end.' And...
Source pages p. 476

Commentary
This ruling-with-proof passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines which verbal formulas constitute a binding oath (yamin mun'aqidah). The author considers the words 'aqsam', 'ahilfu', and 'ashhad' (I swear, I take an oath, I bear witness) even without saying 'billah'. He argues that these words are customarily used for swearing, and the present tense form can be for present or future with a context. The Quranic verses about bearing witness (al-Munafiqun: 1-2) are cited as proof that 'ashhad' can be an oath. The default is that swearing by Allah is legislated; swearing by other than Him is prohibited, so these words are understood to refer to Allah. The author notes that Imam Muhammad in al-Asl listed these as oaths, but they require mention of the thing sworn upon (al-muqsam alayhi) to be binding. Without it, no oath is concluded. There is a difference: some hold that saying 'ala yamin' alone creates an obligation of kaffarah, but this is rejected by most, as kaffarah results from breaking a concluded oath, and without a specific thing sworn upon, there is no basis. The exception is that in Fath al-Qadir, saying 'ala yamin' as an inceptive (insha) is treated like a vow (nadhr) and obligates kaffarah from the start, because it is an undertaking. The passage concludes by distinguishing 'ala yamin' from other formulas like 'half' and 'ashhad', which are not vow formulas and do not create an initial undertaking.

Key Terms
المقسم عليه (al-muqsam ʿalayhi): The thing sworn upon; the action or event that one swears to do or not do.
اليمين الغموس (al-yamīn al-ghamūs): A false oath sworn knowingly about a past event; it is a major sin but no kaffarah.
على يمين (ʿalā yamīn): Expression meaning 'upon me an oath'; considered by some as a vow (nadhr) initiating kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in this passage, what is required for an oath using the phrase 'uhsimu' (I swear) to be binding?
A) Saying the word 'billah'
B) Having the intention to swear
C) Mentioning the thing sworn upon
D) Saying it in the past tense

Key Takeaway
For a binding Hanafi oath using 'aqsam', 'ahilfu', or 'ashhad', you must mention the specific act; 'ala yamin' is exceptional as a vow formula.

The Validity of Swearing by the Life of Allah
Ruling with proof on oath by God's life

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that 'la'amr Allah' is a valid oath and explain why it is based on Allah's attribute of existence.

Core Idea
Swearing by Allah's life is valid because it invokes an attribute of His essence.

The Hanafi school recognizes certain expressions as valid oaths even though they do not explicitly mention the name 'Allah'. One such expression is 'la'amr Allah' (by the life of Allah). This lesson examines the legal reasoning and Quranic evidence behind this ruling, and distinguishes it from invalid forms that involve human actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath formula
Locate the phrase 'la'amr Allah' in the source text. Note its structure: lam + 'amr + Allah. The lam is essential for the oath meaning.
2
Understand the attribute basis
The text explains that 'la'amr Allah' is valid because Allah's existence (baqa') is an attribute of His essence. This makes it analogous to swearing by His power or majesty.
Hanafi usul holds that oaths may be taken by Allah's attributes.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The author cites Surah al-Hijr 15:72 where Allah says 'By your life...' This shows that swearing by life is permissible in revelation, and by analogy applies even more to Allah's eternal life.
The dalala is zanni because it is an analogy, but the thubut is qati.
4
Differentiate invalid forms
The text contrasts 'amrka Allah' (by your acknowledgment of Allah) which is not an oath because it swears by the action of the addressee, not by Allah directly.
This distinction is critical to avoid invalidating an oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كونه حالفاً بقوله لعمر الله فلأن بقاؤه فكان صفة له لأنه من صفة الذات لأنه يوصف به لا بغيره فكأنه قال وبقاء الله كقدرته وكبريائه ولقوله تعالى {لَعَمْرُكَ إِنَّهُمْ لَفِي سَكْرَتِهِمْ يَعْمَهُونَ} [الحجر: ٧٢] هو بالضم والفتح إلا أن الفتح غلب في القسم حتى لا يجوز فيه الضم وارتفاعه على الابتداء وخبره محذوف والخبر قسمي أو يميني؛ كذا في المغرب ولا تلحق المفتوحة الواو في الخط بخلاف عمر والعلم فإنها ألحقت للتفرقة بينه وبين عمر وقيد بكون اللام في أوله لأنه لو لم تدخله اللام فإن القسم فيه محذوف ويكون منصوباً نصب المصادر فتقول عمر الله ما فعلت كما في الله لأفعلن، وأما قولهم عمرك الله ما فعلت فمعناه بإقرارك له بالبقاء، وينبغي أن لا ينعقد يميناً لأنه حلف بفعل المخاطب وهو إقراره واعتقاده كما في فتح القدير. وأما أيم الله فمعناه أيمن الله وهو جميع يمين على قول الأكثر فخفف بالحذف حتى صار أيم الله ثم خففت أيضاً فقيل م الله لأفعلن كذا فتكون ميماً واحدة، وبهذا نفى سبيويه أن يكون جمعاً لأن الجمع لا يبقى على حرف واحد ويقال من الله بضم الميم والنون وفتحهما وكسرهما. وهمزة أيمن بالقطع وإنما وصلت في الوصل تخفيفاً لكثرة الاستعمال ومذهب سيبويه أنها همزة وصل اجتلبت ليمكن بها النطق كهمزة ابن و امرىء من الأسماء الساكنة الأوائل، وإنما كان يميناً لحديث البخاري وايم الله إن كان الخليقاً بالإمارة كما في فتح القدير. وأشار المصنف
And as for the case of his being an oath-swearer by his saying 'la'amr Allah', it is because His existence is an attribute of His self – He is described by it and not by anything else – so it is as if he said 'by the existence of Allah' like His power and His majesty. And for His statement 'By your life, they are in their intoxication wandering blindly' [15:72], it is with damm and fatha, but fatha prevailed in oaths such that damm is not allowed, and it is raised as a subject with its predicate omitted, and the predicate is an oath or a vow. So in al-Mughrib. And the fatha version is not written with a waw in the script, unlike 'Umar and knowledge, because the waw was added to distinguish between 'Umar and 'amr. And it is conditioned that it have the lam at its beginning because if the lam does not enter it, the oath is omitted and it is accusative as a verbal noun; so you say 'amr Allah ma fa'alta' just as 'Allah la af'alanna'. As for their saying 'amrka Allah ma fa'alta', its meaning is 'by your acknowledging His existence', and it should not be concluded as an oath because he swore by the action of the addressee, which is his acknowledgment and belief – as in Fath al-Qadir. And as for 'aym Allah', its meaning is 'ayman Allah', and it is the plural of yamin according to most, then it was lightened by deletion until it became 'aym Allah', then further lightened so it was said 'm Allah la af'alanna katha', becoming a single meem. With this, Sibawayh denied that it is a plural because a plural does not remain on a single letter. And it is said 'man Allah' with damm on the meem and noon, or fatha on both, or kasr on both. And the hamza of 'ayman' is qat', but it is made wasl in conjunctive speech for ease due to frequent use; and Sibawayh's view is that it is a hamza wasl brought to enable pronunciation like the hamza of 'ibn' and 'imri' among nouns with silent first letters. And it is an oath because of the hadith of Bukhari: 'Wa-aym Allah, he was indeed worthy of leadership' as in Fath al-Qadir. And the author indicated.
Source pages p. 477

Commentary
This passage examines the linguistic and legal basis for oaths invoking God's life or eternity. The Hanafi school considers 'la'amr Allah' (by the life of Allah) a valid oath because Allah's existence (baqa') is an attribute of His essence, similar to His power and majesty. The Quranic verse 'By your life' (15:72) is cited as a precedent, showing that swearing by life is established in revelation. The correct pronunciation for oaths is 'la'amr Allah' with fatha on 'amr', not damm (which would be the proper noun 'Umar). The text also explains 'aym Allah' as a contraction of 'ayman Allah' (oaths of Allah), and it is considered an oath due to a hadith in Bukhari. Additionally, 'man Allah' variants are permissible. However, phrases like 'amrka Allah' (your acknowledgment of Allah's existence) are not valid oaths because they involve swearing by the action of the addressee, not by God directly. The discussion highlights the importance of linguistic correctness and the avoidance of using creatures' actions as objects of oaths.

Key Terms
لعمر الله (la'amr Allah): By the life of Allah; an oath formula invoking God's eternal existence.
بقاء (baqa'): Eternal existence of God, an attribute used as basis for swearing by His life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is 'la'amr Allah' considered a valid oath in the Hanafi school?
A) Because it is mentioned in the Quran with the word 'amr'.
B) Because it refers to God's attribute of eternal life, which is like His power and majesty.
C) Because it is a common expression used by people.
D) Because it contains the particle 'la' which indicates an oath.

Key Takeaway
Swearing 'la'amr Allah' is a valid Hanafi oath because it invokes Allah's attribute of eternal life, supported by Quranic analogy and attribute theology.

Derivation and Variants of 'Aym Allah' and Invalid Forms
Linguistic analysis and ruling on oath formulas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify 'aym Allah' as a valid oath, explain its derivation from 'ayman Allah', and recognize permissible linguistic variants.

Core Idea
'Aym Allah' is a valid oath derived from 'ayman Allah', with multiple permissible pronunciations.

Another common oath formula is 'aym Allah' (by the oaths of Allah). The Hanafi school, following hadith and linguistic analysis, accepts this expression. This lesson explores its etymology, the hadith evidence, and the various permissible pronunciations, while also distinguishing it from expressions that are not oaths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the etymology
The text explains that 'aym Allah' is a contraction of 'ayman Allah', which is the plural of 'yamin' (oath). The deletion and lightening occurred due to frequent use.
Sibawayh's view that it is not a plural because it reduces to a single letter (m) is noted.
2
Confirm the hadith evidence
A hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari contains the phrase 'Wa-aym Allah', establishing its usage as an oath by the Prophet or companions.
This provides a sunnah proof for its validity.
3
Evaluate permissible linguistic variants
The text lists variants: 'aym Allah', 'm Allah', 'man Allah' with various vowelizations (damm, fatha, kasr on meem and noon). All are permissible as oaths.
The hamza of 'ayman' is originally qat' but becomes wasl for ease.
4
Distinguish from invalid forms
Reinforce that 'amrka Allah' is invalid, as covered in the previous lesson. 'Aym Allah' is valid because it invokes Allah directly, not a human action.
This connects the two lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما كونه حالفاً بقوله لعمر الله فلأن بقاؤه فكان صفة له لأنه من صفة الذات لأنه يوصف به لا بغيره فكأنه قال وبقاء الله كقدرته وكبريائه ولقوله تعالى {لَعَمْرُكَ إِنَّهُمْ لَفِي سَكْرَتِهِمْ يَعْمَهُونَ} [الحجر: ٧٢] هو بالضم والفتح إلا أن الفتح غلب في القسم حتى لا يجوز فيه الضم وارتفاعه على الابتداء وخبره محذوف والخبر قسمي أو يميني؛ كذا في المغرب ولا تلحق المفتوحة الواو في الخط بخلاف عمر والعلم فإنها ألحقت للتفرقة بينه وبين عمر وقيد بكون اللام في أوله لأنه لو لم تدخله اللام فإن القسم فيه محذوف ويكون منصوباً نصب المصادر فتقول عمر الله ما فعلت كما في الله لأفعلن، وأما قولهم عمرك الله ما فعلت فمعناه بإقرارك له بالبقاء، وينبغي أن لا ينعقد يميناً لأنه حلف بفعل المخاطب وهو إقراره واعتقاده كما في فتح القدير. وأما أيم الله فمعناه أيمن الله وهو جميع يمين على قول الأكثر فخفف بالحذف حتى صار أيم الله ثم خففت أيضاً فقيل م الله لأفعلن كذا فتكون ميماً واحدة، وبهذا نفى سبيويه أن يكون جمعاً لأن الجمع لا يبقى على حرف واحد ويقال من الله بضم الميم والنون وفتحهما وكسرهما. وهمزة أيمن بالقطع وإنما وصلت في الوصل تخفيفاً لكثرة الاستعمال ومذهب سيبويه أنها همزة وصل اجتلبت ليمكن بها النطق كهمزة ابن و امرىء من الأسماء الساكنة الأوائل، وإنما كان يميناً لحديث البخاري وايم الله إن كان الخليقاً بالإمارة كما في فتح القدير. وأشار المصنف

Resolving the Dispute on 'Alayya Yamin': Does It Require Kaffarah?
Critical refinement of the ruling on 'alayya yamin'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'alayya yamin' and evaluate the difference between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A bare 'alayya yamin' without condition still binds you to kaffarah — Abu Hanifa's position prevails.

This lesson tackles a critical intra-Hanafi dispute over the phrase 'alayya yamin' (upon me is an oath). Abu Yusuf held that if not conditioned on anything, no kaffarah is due; Abu Hanifa ruled that kaffarah is required. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supports Abu Hanifa, relying on al-Hawi and textual consistency. You will trace the evidence and understand why the later school adopted the position that 'alayya yamin' is a formula of nadhr (vow) carrying kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Phrase
Find the exact wording 'alayya yamin' and 'alayya nadhr' in the Arabic text. Note that the dispute centers on whether a condition (ta'liq) is necessary for the phrase to be a binding oath.
The text contrasts 'alayya yamin' with conditional oaths like 'in fa'altu katha fa-alayya yamin'.
2
State the Two Positions
Abu Yusuf: no kaffarah if not conditioned. Abu Hanifa: kaffarah is due regardless of condition, because the expression itself is a formula of vow (sigha al-nadhr) that obligates kaffarah.
The author sides with Abu Hanifa, calling it 'ta'yid' (support) for his narration.
3
Examine the Proof (Dalil)
The author cites al-Hawi: 'Alayya nadhr or alayya yamin without conditioning it, then upon him is kaffarah yamin.' This aligns with Abu Hanifa. The reasoning: these expressions are from the formulas of nadhr, which trigger kaffarah even without a subject.
No external Quranic/hadith evidence is quoted for this specific ruling; it is based on legal maxims and consensus of the later school.
4
Understand the Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
If a person says 'alayya yamin' and later does not fulfill an implied obligation, is kaffarah due? Under Abu Hanifa, yes; under Abu Yusuf, no. The mufta bihi (operative position) is Abu Hanifa's.
This affects fatwa and personal accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وبه اندفع ما في فتح القدير أقول : فيه نظر فإن المتبادر مما في المجتبى اختلاف الرواية وذلك أنه قال ما نصه : ولو قال علي يمين أو يمين الله فيمين. ثم قال أي صاحب الرمز المذكور : علي يمين يريد به الإيجاب لا كفارة عليه إذا لم يعلقه بشيء، وكذا إذا قال الله علي يمين، هكذا روي عن أبي يوسف. وعن أبي حنيفة علي يمين لا كفارة لها يريد الإيجاب فعليه يمين لها كفارة اهـ. ما في المجتبى. وذكر في الحاوي ما نصه : علي نذر أو علي يمين ولم يعلقه فعليه كفارة يمين فهذا ما قاله في الفتح. وإذا كان علي يمين من صيغ النذر كما قال في الفتح لم يظهر فرق بين علي نذر وعلي يمين فلذا قال في الفتح الحق أنه مثله فهذا تأييد للرواية المروية عن أبي حنيفة فافهم قوله : (إلا إذا قصد غير اليمين) فيدين رأيت في هامش بعض النسخ أقول : حق العبارة لا يكون صريحا إلى أنه لو قال يمين الله لا أفعلن كذا فهو يمين صرح به في المجتبى. وأما كونه حالفا بعهد الله وميثاقه فلأن العهد في الأصل هي المواعدة التي تكون بين اثنين لوثوق أحدهما على الآخر وهو الميثاق وقد استعمل في اليمين لقوله تعالى {وَأَوْفُوا بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ إِذَا عَاهَدْتُمْ} [النحل: 91] الآية، فقد جعل العهد في القرآن يمينا كما ترى والميثاق في معناه، وكذا الحلف بالذمة ولذا يسمى الذمي معاهداً. وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا لم ينو لغلبة الاستعمال للعهد، والميثاق في معنى اليمين فينصرفان إليه إلا إذا قصد غير اليمين فيدين. وفي الذخيرة: لو قال إن فعلت كذا فعلي يمين إن شاء فلان ففعل ذلك الفعل وشاء فلان لزمه كما قال، وأما كونه حالفا بقوله علي نذر ونذر الله فيشترط أن يذكر المحلوف عليه لكونها يمينا منعقدة نحو يقول علي نذر الله لأفعلن كذا أو لا أفعل كذا حتى إذا لم يف بما حلف عليه لزمته كفارة اليمين، وأما إذا لم يذكر شيئا بأن قال علي نذر الله فإنه لا يكون يمينا لأن اليمين إنما تتحقق لمحلوف عليه ولكن تلزمه الكفارة فيكون هذا التزام الكفارة ابتداء بهذه العبارة؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وهذا كله إذا لم ينو بهذا النذر المطلق شيئا من القرب كحج أو صوم، فإن كان نوى بقوله علي نذر إن فعلت كذا قربة مقصودة يصح النذر بها ففعل لزمته تلك القربة لما ذكره الحاكم بقوله : فإن حلف بالنذر فإن نوى شيئا من حج أو عمرة فعليه ما نوى، وإن لم يكن له نية فعليه كفارة اليمين ا هـ . فيحمل الحديث من نذر نذرا لم خلط الزيلعي مسألة لفظ النذر بصيغة النذر وبينهما فرق تطلع عليه إن شاء الله. وفي الولوالجية وغيرها : لو قال الله علي أن لا أكلم فلاناً أنها ليست بيمين إلا أن ينوي لأن الصيغة احتمال معنى اليمين ا هـ . وأما مسألة الحلف بالتعليق بالكفر فلأنه لما جعل الشرط للذر مع يميناً كما في النهر لما قاله شيخنا إن الإيمان لا تدخل تحت القضاء حتى يكون للديانة فيها مدخل تأمل. وبدليل ما سيأتي تحت قوله ولو زاد ثوباً الخ ) حيث قال : اعلم أن الفرق بين الديانة والقضاء إنما يظهر في الطلاق والعتاق وأما في الحلف بالله تعالى فلا يظهر لأن الكفارة حق الله تعالى ليس للعبد فيها حق حتى يرفع الحالف إلى القاضي .اهـ. قلت : قد يقال إنه يمكن أن يترتب عليها حق عبد كما لو علق طلاقا أو عتاقا على حلفه ثم حلف بذلك وقال قصدت غير اليمين فلا يصدق قضاء بل يدين .
His statement: (And with it is repelled what is in Fath al-Qadir) I say: There is consideration in it, because what is apparent from al-Mujtaba is difference of narration. He said his text: 'If he says 'alayya yamin' or 'yamin Allah', it is an oath.' Then he said, i.e., the author of the mentioned sign: 'Alayya yamin meaning obligation, no kaffarah upon him if he did not condition it on something, and similarly if he says 'Allah alayya yamin'. Thus it is narrated from Abu Yusuf. And from Abu Hanifa: 'Alayya yamin with no kaffarah on it meaning obligation, then upon him is an oath with kaffarah.' End of what is in al-Mujtaba. And in al-Hawi he said his text: 'Alayya nadhr or alayya yamin and he did not condition it, then upon him is kaffarah yamin.' So this is what he said in al-Fath. And if 'alayya yamin' is from the formulas of nadhr as he said in al-Fath, then no difference appears between 'alayya nadhr' and 'alayya yamin', so he said in al-Fath the truth is that it is like it, so this is a support for the narration from Abu Hanifa. Understand. His statement: (Except if he intended other than an oath) then he is believed in private. I saw in the margin of some copies I say: The correct expression is not explicit to the effect that if he says 'yamin Allah la af'alanna katha' then it is an oath – stated in al-Mujtaba. And as for his being an oath-swearer by 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaqah', because 'ahd' originally is the mutual promise between two people for one's trust in the other, and it is al-mithaq, and it has been used for an oath due to His statement 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted' [16:91], so the covenant is made an oath in the Quran as you see, and al-mithaq is in its meaning. Similarly, swearing by al-dhimmah, and therefore the dhimmi is called mu'ahad. And he made it unrestricted so it includes when he does not intend, due to the prevalence of use for 'ahd', and mithaq is in the meaning of an oath, so they both refer to it, except if he intended other than an oath – then he is believed in private. And in al-Dhakhira: If he said 'If I do such and such, then upon me is an oath if so-and-so wills', then he did the action and so-and-so willed, it becomes binding as he said. And as for his being an oath-swearer by his saying 'alayya nadhr' and 'nadhr Allah', it is required that he mention the subject of the oath for it to be a binding oath, like he says 'alayya nadhr Allah la af'alanna katha' or 'la af'al katha' so that if he does not fulfill what he swore upon, kaffarah yamin becomes due. And if he does not mention anything, by saying 'alayya nadhr Allah', then it is not an oath because an oath only occurs for a subject, but kaffarah becomes due upon him, so this is a commitment to kaffarah initially by this expression; as in Fath al-Qadir. All this is if he did not intend by this absolute nadhr any act of worship like hajj or fasting. If he intended by his saying 'alayya nadhr in fa'altu katha' a specific act of worship that is valid for nadhr, then he did it, that act of worship becomes due upon him, because of what al-Hakim mentioned: 'If he swears by nadhr, then if he intended something of hajj or umrah, what he intended is due; and if he had no intention, then kaffarah yamin is due.' End quote. So the hadith 'Whoever makes a nadhr without naming it, its kaffarah is the kaffarah of an oath' is interpreted as the case when he had no intention. And the restriction to 'lafz al-nadhr' excludes 'sigha al-nadhr', like saying 'Allah alayya katha salat rak'atayn' or absolutely, 'sawm yawmayn' without condition or conditioned as will come soon. And al-Zayla'i mixed the issue of 'lafz al-nadhr' and 'sigha al-nadhr', but there is a difference between them, which you will see if Allah wills. And in al-Walwaljiya and others: If he says 'Allah alayya an la ukallima fulanan', it is not an oath except if he intends, because the formula has the possibility of oath meaning. End quote. And as for the issue of swearing by conditioning on kufr, because he made the condition... as in al-Nahr, because our teacher said: 'Faith is not subject to qada' such that diyanah has an entry in it – ponder.' And by the evidence of what will come under his statement 'and if he increases a garment, etc.' where he said: 'Know that the difference between diyanah and qada' only appears in divorce and manumission; as for swearing by Allah the Exalted, it does not appear because kaffarah is a right of Allah, the slave has no right in it such that the swearer would take his case to the judge.' End quote. I say: It can be said that a slave's right can be attached to it, as if he conditioned divorce or manumission on his oath, then he swore by that and said 'I intended other than an oath', then he is not believed in court but is believed in private.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that addresses several issues in oaths. First, it reconciles a seeming contradiction in al-Mujtaba regarding 'alayya yamin': Abu Yusuf says no kaffarah if not conditioned, while Abu Hanifa says kaffarah is due. The author sides with Abu Hanifa, supported by al-Hawi, and concludes that 'alayya yamin' is like a vow and requires kaffarah. Then it discusses oaths by 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaqah' (covenant of God), citing Quran 16:91 to show that these are valid oaths in the Hanafi school, as they are used in the Quran for swearing. The default is that they are oaths unless one intends otherwise (then he is believed in private). The passage also covers the conditional oath: if one says 'upon me is an oath if so-and-so wills', the oath binds only if the condition is met. Next, it explains the vow 'alayya nadhr': it is a binding oath only if a subject (mahluuf 'alayh) is mentioned; otherwise it is a commitment to kaffarah but not an oath. If the vow is for a specific act of worship (hajj, fasting), then that act becomes obligatory, not kaffarah. A key distinction is made between the word 'nadhr' (lafz al-nadhr) and the formula of vow (sigha al-nadhr), which al-Zayla'i confused. Finally, the issue of swearing conditional on kufr is addressed, along with the distinction between diyanah (private belief) and qada' (court judgment): in matters of kaffarah, the judge does not investigate intention because it is a right of God, but if a slave's right (e.g., divorce) is attached, then intention matters in court as well.

Key Terms
علي يمين (alayya yamin): Upon me is an oath; a phrase used to swear an oath.
علي نذر (alayya nadhr): Upon me is a vow; a commitment to do an act of worship or to pay kaffarah.
محلوف عليه (mahluuf 'alayh): The subject of the oath; what is sworn upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling when someone says 'Alayya yamin' without any condition or subject?
A) It is not an oath and no kaffarah is due.
B) It is a valid oath requiring kaffarah if broken.
C) It is a vow that must be fulfilled by a specific act of worship.
D) It is invalid because the subject is missing.

Key Takeaway
A bare 'alayya yamin' is treated as a vow (nadhr) requiring kaffarah upon non-fulfillment, per the settled Hanafi position.

Oaths by the Covenant of Allah: The Ruling on 'Ahd and Mithaq
Proof of validity of oaths by 'ahd' and 'mithaq' with diyanah/qada' distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why swearing by 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaq' are valid oaths and distinguish between diyanah and qada' in oath cases.

Core Idea
Swearing by God's covenant is an oath, and your intention matters privately but not in court for kaffarah.

This lesson focuses on expressions of oath using 'ahd Allah' (covenant of God) and 'mithaq' (pact). The author proves their validity by citing Quran 16:91, where 'ahd' is used in the context of oaths. You will also learn the crucial distinction between diyanah (private belief) and qada' (court judgment): for kaffarah, the judge does not investigate intention because it is a divine right, but if a human right is attached (e.g., divorce), intention matters in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath Formulas
In the Arabic text, find the expressions: 'bi-ahd Allah' and 'bi-mithaqih' (by the covenant of God and His pact). Note that the author says 'wa amma kawnuhu halifan bi-'ahd Allah' indicating these are valid oath formulas.
Also note that 'dhimmah' is similar, and its derivative 'mu'ahad' shows the communal aspect.
2
Examine the Quranic Proof
The author cites Quran 16:91: 'Wa awfu bi-'ahd Allah idha 'ahadtum' (And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted). The phrase 'ahd' is clearly used here for a covenant that includes swearing. The author argues that since the Quran treats 'ahd' as an oath, swearing by 'ahd Allah' is a valid oath in Islamic law.
The dalil is explicit: the verse commands fulfillment of the covenant, which parallels the obligation to fulfill an oath.
3
Understand the Role of Intention (Diyanah)
The text states that these expressions are predominantly used as oaths, so they are taken as oaths unless the speaker intended something else (qasada ghayr al-yamin). If the speaker had another intention, he is believed in private (diyanah) but not in court (qada') because kaffarah is a divine right.
This is a key principle: for acts concerning purely divine rights, the judge does not probe intention.
4
Distinguish Diyanah from Qada'
The passage later clarifies: the difference between diyanah and qada' appears in divorce and manumission (where human rights are involved), but for oaths by Allah, intention is not investigated in court because kaffarah is a right of Allah. However, if a human right (like divorce) is attached to the oath, then intention becomes relevant in court.
This nuance affects fatwa and judicial practice.

His statement: (And with it is repelled what is in Fath al-Qadir) I say: There is consideration in it, because what is apparent from al-Mujtaba is difference of narration. He said his text: 'If he says 'alayya yamin' or 'yamin Allah', it is an oath.' Then he said, i.e., the author of the mentioned sign: 'Alayya yamin meaning obligation, no kaffarah upon him if he did not condition it on something, and similarly if he says 'Allah alayya yamin'. Thus it is narrated from Abu Yusuf. And from Abu Hanifa: 'Alayya yamin with no kaffarah on it meaning obligation, then upon him is an oath with kaffarah.' End of what is in al-Mujtaba. And in al-Hawi he said his text: 'Alayya nadhr or alayya yamin and he did not condition it, then upon him is kaffarah yamin.' So this is what he said in al-Fath. And if 'alayya yamin' is from the formulas of nadhr as he said in al-Fath, then no difference appears between 'alayya nadhr' and 'alayya yamin', so he said in al-Fath the truth is that it is like it, so this is a support for the narration from Abu Hanifa. I saw in the margin of some copies I say: The correct expression is not explicit to the effect that if he says 'yamin Allah la af'alanna katha' then it is an oath – stated in al-Mujtaba. And as for his being an oath-swearer by 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaqah', because 'ahd' originally is the mutual promise between two people for one's trust in the other, and it is al-mithaq, and it has been used for an oath due to His statement 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted' [16:91], so the covenant is made an oath in the Quran as you see, and al-mithaq is in its meaning. Similarly, swearing by al-dhimmah, and therefore the dhimmi is called mu'ahad. And he made it unrestricted so it includes when he does not intend, due to the prevalence of use for 'ahd', and mithaq is in the meaning of an oath, so they both refer to it, except if he intended other than an oath – then he is believed in private. And in al-Dhakhira: If he said 'If I do such and such, then upon me is an oath if so-and-so wills', then he did the action and so-and-so willed, it becomes binding as he said. And as for his being an oath-swearer by his saying 'alayya nadhr' and 'nadhr Allah', it is required that he mention the subject of the oath for it to be a binding oath, like he says 'alayya nadhr Allah la af'alanna katha' or 'la af'al katha' so that if he does not fulfill what he swore upon, kaffarah yamin becomes due. And if he does not mention anything, by saying 'alayya nadhr Allah', then it is not an oath because an oath only occurs for a subject, but kaffarah becomes due upon him, so this is a commitment to kaffarah initially by this expression; as in Fath al-Qadir. All this is if he did not intend by this absolute nadhr any act of worship like hajj or fasting. If he intended by his saying 'alayya nadhr in fa'altu katha' a specific act of worship that is valid for nadhr, then he did it, that act of worship becomes due upon him, because of what al-Hakim mentioned: 'If he swears by nadhr, then if he intended something of hajj or umrah, what he intended is due; and if he had no intention, then kaffarah yamin is due.' End quote. So the hadith 'Whoever makes a nadhr without naming it, its kaffarah is the kaffarah of an oath' is interpreted as the case when he had no intention. And the restriction to 'lafz al-nadhr' excludes 'sigha al-nadhr', like saying 'Allah alayya katha salat rak'atayn' or absolutely, 'sawm yawmayn' without condition or conditioned as will come soon. And al-Zayla'i mixed the issue of 'lafz al-nadhr' and 'sigha al-nadhr', but there is a difference between them, which you will see if Allah wills. And in al-Walwaljiya and others: If he says 'Allah alayya an la ukallima fulanan', it is not an oath except if he intends, because the formula has the possibility of oath meaning. End quote. And as for the issue of swearing by conditioning on kufr, because he made the condition... as in al-Nahr, because our teacher said: 'Faith is not subject to qada' such that diyanah has an entry in it – ponder.' And by the evidence of what will come under his statement 'and if he increases a garment, etc.' where he said: 'Know that the difference between diyanah and qada' only appears in divorce and manumission; as for swearing by Allah the Exalted, it does not appear because kaffarah is a right of Allah, the slave has no right in it such that the swearer would take his case to the judge.' End quote. I say: It can be said that a slave's right can be attached to it, as if he conditioned divorce or manumission on his oath, then he swore by that and said 'I intended other than an oath', then he is not believed in court but is believed in private.
Source pages p. 478

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that addresses several issues in oaths. First, it reconciles a seeming contradiction in al-Mujtaba regarding 'alayya yamin': Abu Yusuf says no kaffarah if not conditioned, while Abu Hanifa says kaffarah is due. The author sides with Abu Hanifa, supported by al-Hawi, and concludes that 'alayya yamin' is like a vow and requires kaffarah. Then it discusses oaths by 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaqah' (covenant of God), citing Quran 16:91 to show that these are valid oaths in the Hanafi school, as they are used in the Quran for swearing. The default is that they are oaths unless one intends otherwise (then he is believed in private). The passage also covers the conditional oath: if one says 'upon me is an oath if so-and-so wills', the oath binds only if the condition is met. Next, it explains the vow 'alayya nadhr': it is a binding oath only if a subject (mahluuf 'alayh) is mentioned; otherwise it is a commitment to kaffarah but not an oath. If the vow is for a specific act of worship (hajj, fasting), then that act becomes obligatory, not kaffarah. A key distinction is made between the word 'nadhr' (lafz al-nadhr) and the formula of vow (sigha al-nadhr), which al-Zayla'i confused. Finally, the issue of swearing conditional on kufr is addressed, along with the distinction between diyanah (private belief) and qada' (court judgment): in matters of kaffarah, the judge does not investigate intention because it is a right of God, but if a slave's right (e.g., divorce) is attached, then intention matters in court as well.

Key Terms
عهد الله (ahd Allah): Covenant of God; used as an oath formula based on Quranic usage.
ميثاق (mithaq): Pact or covenant; synonymous with 'ahd in oath usage.
ديانة وقضاء (diyanah wa qada'): Private belief vs. court judgment; the former governs intention in oaths, the latter only when a slave's right is at stake.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider 'ahd Allah' a valid oath formula?
A) Because of a hadith of the Prophet.
B) Because the Quran uses 'ahd' in the context of oaths.
C) Because of consensus among all schools.
D) Because it is explicitly mentioned in the Fath al-Qadir.

Key Takeaway
Swearing by God's covenant ('ahd Allah') is a valid oath, and intention is only considered privately, not in court for kaffarah.
