Kaffarah Before Breaking the Oath is Invalid
Ruling of invalidity of premature kaffarah with ta'lil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on expiating an oath before breaking it and explain the rationale based on the text.

Core Idea
Kaffarah covers an offense; no offense before the oath is broken, so early expiation is merely charity.

This lesson addresses a common question: can one expiate an oath before actually breaking it? The Hanafi school rules clearly that such expiation is invalid and counts only as charity. We'll explore the reasoning behind this ruling and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is performing kaffarah (expiation) before breaking the oath (al-hanth). The ruling is that it is invalid (batil) as kaffarah.
The text directly states: 'ولا يكفر قبل الحنث أي لا يصح التكفير قبل الحنث'.
2
Understand the legal rationale (ta'lil)
The reason is that kaffarah is meant to 'cover the offense' (jirm nahiy). Before the oath is broken, there is no offense. The oath itself is a deterrent, not a cause for expiation.
The phrase 'لأن الكفارة لستر الجناية ولا جناية' is key.
3
Distinguish from the wound case
The text contrasts with expiation for a fatal wound before death. In that case, expiation is valid because the wound is a likely cause of death, whereas an oath prevents the sin.
The analogy (qiyas) shows that cause/effect matters; the oath does not lead to the sin.
4
Apply the consequence
If one has given property for kaffarah prematurely, it cannot be reclaimed from the poor because it is considered a valid charity (sadaqah).
The text: 'ثم إذا كفر قبله لا يسترده من الفقير لوقوعه صدقة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا يكفر قبل الحنث أي لا يصح التكفير قبل الحنث في اليمين، سواء كان بالمال أو بالصوم، لأن الكفارة لستر الجناية ولا جناية واليمين ليست بسبب لأنها مانعة من الحنث غير مغضية إليه بخلاف التكفير بعد الجرح قبل الموت لأنه مفض، ثم إذا كفر قبله لا يسترده من الفقير لوقوعه صدقة. ولم يذكر المصنف مسألة تعداد الكفارة لتعدد اليمين وهي مهمة.
His statement: 'And do not expiate before the sin' means that expiation before breaking the oath is not valid, whether with property or fasting, because the expiation is to cover the offense, and there is no offense (yet). The oath is not a cause because it prevents the sin, not leading to it, contrary to expiation after a wound but before death because that leads (to death). Then if he expiates before (the sin), he cannot take it back from the poor because it has occurred as charity. And the author did not mention the issue of multiple expiations due to multiple oaths, and it is important.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage clarifies a fundamental rule: kaffarah (expiation) for an oath is only valid after the oath is broken (al-hinth). Performing kaffarah before breaking the oath is invalid, whether it involves property (freeing, feeding, clothing) or fasting. The reason (ta'lil) is that kaffarah is meant to 'cover' (sitr) the offense of breaking the oath. Before the break, there is no offense. The oath itself is not a cause for expiation because its natural function is to prevent sin (it is a deterrent), not to lead to it. This differs from the case of a fatal wound: expiation for murder can be made before death because the wound leads to death. If someone mistakenly gives property for expiation before breaking the oath, it is considered a voluntary charity (sadaqah) and cannot be reclaimed from the poor recipient. The author notes that the topic of multiple expiations for multiple oaths is not covered here but is important (to be discussed later).

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): The act of breaking an oath; the point at which expiation becomes obligatory.
جناية (jināyah): Offense or violation; here, breaking the oath is considered a minor 'offense' that requires expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is kaffarah before breaking an oath invalid according to this passage?
A) Because the oath is not yet broken, so there is no offense to cover.
B) Because kaffarah must only be done after death.
C) Because expiation before is always a sin.
D) Because the poor might be undeserving.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for an oath is only valid after the oath is broken; premature expiation is invalid and becomes charity.

Multiple Oaths with Multiple Names of Allah: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on multiplicity of oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the majority (zahir al-riwaya) and minority (Hasan from Abu Hanifa) positions on whether multiple divine names with separate waw create multiple oaths, and apply the ruling to similar expressions.

Core Idea
Repeating the oath particle (waw) with each divine name creates separate oaths; doubt from a single waw prevents multiplicity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical question: if a person swears by multiple names of Allah (e.g., Wallah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim), does he incur one oath or several? The answer depends on whether the waw (the oath particle) is repeated. This intra-Hanafi dispute illustrates how the school weighs evidence from zahir al-riwaya against weaker narrations. Understanding this ruling will help you determine the number of expiations (kaffarat) required when such oaths are broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The act is swearing by multiple divine names (e.g., Wallah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim) with a waw before each. The dispute: does this create one oath or multiple oaths?
The outcome affects how many expiations are due upon breaking the oath.
2
State the majority (zahir al-riwaya) position
The majority of Hanafi scholars, relying on the apparent narrations (zahir al-riwaya), hold that each name with a separate waw constitutes a separate oath, requiring multiple expiations.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
3
State the minority (Hasan from Abu Hanifa) position
A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa required only one expiation. The scholars of Samarkand adopted this view.
This is a weaker (nawadir) transmission, not part of zahir al-riwaya.
4
Analyse the evidential basis and the distinction
The majority argues that the repetition of the waw explicitly indicates independent oaths. The minority argues that a single waw could be conjunctive, and doubt prevents establishing multiple oaths. When the waw is repeated (e.g., Wallah, Wallah), both sides agree on multiplicity.
The condition for multiplicity is the repetition of the oath particle (waw).
5
Apply the rule to other scenarios
The passage extends the principle: repeated oaths in one or multiple gatherings generally create multiple expiations unless the second is intended as repetition of the first. The same logic applies in divorce: repeated conditional pronouncements produce multiple divorces.
This confirms the general Hanafi inclination toward multiplicity when expressions are repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الظهيرية : ولو قال والله والرحمن والرحيم لا أفعل كذا ففعل ففي الروايات الظاهرة يلزمه ثلاث كفارات ويتعدد اليمين بتعدد الاسم لكن يشترط تخلل حرف القسم، واحد من الثلاث لا فعلى هذا يكون أحد دائراً كما أشار إليه بقوله الإعتاق والإطعام والكسوة يصوم فبطل اعتراض من اعترض عليه والله تعالى أعلم.  وفي روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن عليه كفارة واحدة وبه أخذ مشايخ سمرقند وأكثر المشايخ على ظاهر الرواية ولو قال والله والرحمن لا أفعل كذا ففعل يلزمه كفارتان في قولهم جميعاً. والفرق على قول أولئك المشايخ أن الواو إذا اتحد ذكره يحتمل أن تكون واو عطف، ويحتمل أن تكون واو القسم، ولا يثبت القسم بالشك والاحتمال بخلاف ما إذا تعدد ذكره لأن أحدهما للعطف والآخر للقسم. ولو قال والله والله يتعدد اليمين في ظاهر الرواية، وروى ابن سماعة محمد أن. عن في الاسم الواحد لا يتعدد اليمين ولو قال والله الله أو قال والله الرحمن تكون يميناً واحدة ا هـ . وفي الولوالجية : إذا أدخل بين اسمين حرف عطف كانا يمينين، وإن كان بغير حرف العطف كان على سبيل الصفة والتأكيد تكون يميناً واحدة ا هـ . الخلاصة معزياً إلى الأصل : إذا حلف على أمر أن لا يفعله ثم حلف في ذلك المجلس أو في مجلس آخر أن لا يفعله أبداً ثم فعله إن نوى يميناً مبتدأ أو التشديد أو لم ينو فعليه كفارة يمينين، أما إذا نوى بالثاني الأول فعليه كفارة واحدة وفي التجريد عن أبي حنيفة : إذا حلف بأيمان فعليه لكل يمين كفارة والمجلس والمجالس سواء، ولو قال عنيت بالثاني الأول لم ذلك في اليمين بالله تعالى، ولو حلف بحجة أو عمرة يستقيم. وفي الأصل أيضاً: ولو قال هو يهودي هو نصراني إن فعل كذا يمين ،واحدة ولو قال هو يهودي إن فعل كذا نصراني إن فعل كذا فهما يمينان في النوازل : قال لآخر والله لا أكلمه يوماً والله لا أكلمه شهراً والله لا أكلمه سنة ، إن كلمه بعد ساعة فعليه ثلاثة ،أيمان وإن كلمه بعد الغد فعليه یمینان وإن كلمه بعد الشهر فعليه يمين ،واحدة، وإن كلمه بعد سنة فلا شيء عليه ا هـ . وفي فتح القدير : وعرف في الطلاق أنه لو قال لها إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق فدخلت وقع ثلاث تطليقات .
He said in al-Zahiriyyah: 'If one says, "By Allah, by the Most Merciful, by the Compassionate, I will not do such-and-such" and then does it, then in the apparent narrations, three expiations are required, and the oath is multiplied by the multiplicity of the names, but on condition that the particle of oath (waw) intervenes; one of the three is not ... so on this, one is revolving...' as he indicated by his saying: 'freeing, feeding, clothing, he fasts' so the objection of whoever objected to him is invalid, and Allah knows best. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that only one expiation is required, and the scholars of Samarkand adopted this, while most scholars adopt the apparent narration. If he said 'By Allah, by the Most Merciful, I will not do such-and-such' and then does it, two expiations are required according to all of them. The distinction according to those scholars is that when the waw is mentioned once, it could be a conjunction (waw al-'atf) or a particle of oath (waw al-qasm), and an oath is not established by doubt. Contrary to when it is mentioned twice, because one is for conjunction and the other for oath. If he says 'By Allah, by Allah' the oath multiplies in the apparent narration, and Ibn Samā'ah narrated from Muhammad that in the single name it does not multiply. And if he says 'By Allah Allah' or 'By Allah the Most Merciful' it is one oath. In al-Walwalijiyyah: If he inserts a conjunction between two names, they are two oaths; without a conjunction, it is for description and emphasis, so it is one oath. Al-Khulāsa citing the Asl: If he swears not to do a thing, then swears again in the same gathering or another not to do it forever, then does it, if he intended a new oath or emphasis or did not intend, two expiations are due; but if he intended the second to mean the first, then one expiation. In al-Tajrīd from Abu Hanifa: If he swears by multiple oaths, each oath has its own expiation, whether in one gathering or multiple. And if he says 'I meant the second to be the first', that is not accepted in oaths by Allah. And if he swears by Hajj or Umrah, it is valid. In al-Asl also: If he says 'He is a Jew, he is a Christian, if he does such-and-such' it is one oath. If he says 'He is a Jew if he does such-and-such, a Christian if he does such-and-such' then they are two oaths. In al-Nawāzil: He said to another, 'By Allah I will not speak to him for a day, By Allah I will not speak to him for a month, By Allah I will not speak to him for a year'; if he speaks after an hour, three expiations are due; if after a day, two expiations; if after a month, one expiation; if after a year, nothing. In Fath al-Qadīr: It is known in divorce that if he says to her, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, if you enter the house, you are divorced, if you enter the house, you are divorced' and she enters, three divorces occur.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage covers the complex rulings on multiple oaths, especially when multiple names of Allah are invoked. The main point of dispute (intra-Hanafi) is: if someone says 'Wallah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim' with the waw (and) between them, does it create three oaths or one? The apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from the core texts holds that each name creates a separate oath, requiring three expiations, as long as the oath particle (waw) is repeated. However, a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa said only one expiation is required. The scholars of Samarkand followed this view, but the majority (jumuhr) stick to the apparent narration. When two names are used (e.g., Wallah wa al-Rahman), all agree two expiations are due. The reasoning: if the waw is used only once, it could be conjunctive or emphatic, so doubt prevents establishing two oaths; but if the waw is repeated (e.g., Wallah, Wallah), it clearly indicates two oaths. Without a conjunction (e.g., Wallah Allah), it is considered emphatic and one oath. Al-Walwalijiyyah summarizes: with a conjunction = two oaths; without = one. The passage then extends to other scenarios: repeating the same oath in one or multiple gatherings: if the second oath is intended as new or for emphasis, two expiations; if one intends it as repetition of the first, only one. But in oaths by Allah, one cannot simply claim the second was meant to be the first (that is not accepted). Swearing by Hajj or Umrah is valid (counts as an oath). Different wordings like 'he is a Jew' etc. are considered one oath unless separate conditions are linked. A practical example: swearing not to speak for different durations (day, month, year) results in multiple expiations depending on when the oath is broken. Finally, a parallel rule in divorce: repeated conditional divorces in one sentence trigger multiple divorces. This establishes the principle of multiplicity (ta'addud) based on expression.

Key Terms
واو القسم (wāw al-qasam): The letter waw used to introduce an oath, equivalent to 'by'.
ظاهر الرواية (zāhir al-riwāyah): The well-known and authoritative narrations from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.
حسن عن أبي حنيفة (Hasan 'an Abi Hanīfah): A narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad (a student of Abu Hanifa) attributed to the imam, considered a weaker (nawādir) transmission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), if someone swears 'Wallah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim' (with two waw), how many expiations are required?
A) One expiation because all names refer to the same being.
B) Three expiations because each name with a waw constitutes a separate oath.
C) Two expiations because the third name is an adjective.
D) None unless the oath is broken three times.

Key Takeaway
When swearing by multiple divine names, the repetition of the oath particle (waw) determines multiplicity; the Hanafi majority holds that each repeated waw creates a separate oath, requiring multiple expiations.

Breaking an Oath of Sin: The Obligation to Break It
Ruling that breaking a sinful oath is obligatory, with hadith proof and a distinction on time-limited oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on an oath involving sin, explain the hadith proof, and distinguish between affirmative and negative oaths in this context.

Core Idea
Breaking a sinful oath is wajib — obedience to Allah overrides the oath.

We often assume that oaths must always be kept to avoid the sin of perjury. But Islamic law prioritizes obedience to Allah over reverence for an oath. This lesson explains that if an oath leads to sin, the law requires you to break it and then perform expiation (kaffarah). The source passage is from a Hanafi commentary that establishes this ruling with clear hadith evidence and a subtle distinction about time-limited oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text discusses swearing to commit a sin (ma'siyah) or to abandon an obligation (fard). The ruling is that breaking the oath (hanth) is obligatory (wajib). The phrase 'yanbaghi an yahinth' (it is proper to break) is interpreted as 'wajib' in this context.
The word 'yanbaghi' often carries a nuance of recommendation, but in the context of sinful oaths, the Hanafi scholars elevate it to obligation based on the hadith.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text cites the hadith from Aisha (ra) in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'Whoever vows to obey Allah, let him obey Him; whoever vows to disobey Allah, let him not disobey Him.' This is the primary evidence. The same principle applies to oaths (yamīn) as to vows (nathr).
The hadith is quoted partially in the source; the full text is: «من نذر أن يطيع الله فليطعه، ومن نذر أن يعصي الله فلا يعصه»
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof yields the ruling)
The imperative 'fa la ya'sih' (let him not disobey) is a command. In Hanafi usul, a command (amr) indicates obligation (wujub) unless there is evidence to the contrary. Thus, it is obligatory to avoid the sinful act itself, which means breaking the oath that leads to it.
The hadith uses the word 'vow' (nathr), but the text applies it to oaths by way of analogy or direct inclusion, as the underlying principle is that one must not persist in disobedience.
4
Note the distinction: time-limited oaths
The text adds a nuance: the condition that an oath must be 'limited by time' (muwaqqatah) applies only to affirmative oaths (e.g., 'I will do such-and-such'), not to negative oaths (e.g., 'I will not pray'). For negative sinful oaths, breaking the oath can occur at any time before death.
This distinction is important for practical application. For example, if someone swears not to pray, he can break the oath by praying at any time, and no time limit is needed for the oath to be applicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن حلف على معصية ينبغي أن يحنث بيان لبعض أحكام اليمين وحاصلها أن المحلوف عليه أنواع : فعل معصية أو ترك فرض، فالحنث واجب وهو المراد بقوله ينبغي أن يحنث أي يجب عليه الحنث الحديث البخاري عن عائشة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم «من نذر أن يطيع الله فليطعه، ومن نذر أن يعصي الله فلا يعصه» (۱) وحديث البخاري أيضاً «وإذا حلفت على يمين قوله : ( ولا بد أن تكون اليمين مؤقتة بوقت الخ) هذا خاص بالثاني أعني الإثبات، أما النفي مثل لا يصلي فيتصور الحنث قبل موته بأن يصلي . (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الأيمان باب ۲۸ ۳۱ أبو داود في كتاب الأيمان باب ١٩. الترمذي في كتاب النذور باب ٢. النسائي في كتاب الأيمان باب ۲۷ ۲۸ الموطأ في كتاب الكفارات حديث ۸، أحمد في مسنده (٣٦/٦، ٤١).
His statement: 'And whoever swears to commit a sin, it is proper that he break the oath' is an explanation of some rulings of oaths. The summary is that the object of the oath is of types: committing a sin or abandoning an obligation, so breaking the oath is obligatory. That is the meaning of 'it is proper that he break' i.e., it is obligatory upon him to break the oath. The hadith of Bukhari from Aisha from the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'Whoever vows to obey Allah, let him obey Him; whoever vows to disobey Allah, let him not disobey Him.' And also the hadith of Bukhari: 'And if you swear an oath...' His statement: 'And it is necessary that the oath be limited by time...' is specific to the second, i.e., affirmation. As for negation, like 'I will not pray', breaking it before his death can be imagined by his praying.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage explains one of the most important practical rulings regarding oaths: if someone swears to do something that is sinful (ma'siyah) or to abandon something obligatory (fard/wajib), it is actually obligatory (wajib) to break the oath. The phrase 'it is proper that he break' (yanbaghi an yahinth) means 'it is obligatory upon him to break'. This is based on the hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari: 'Whoever vows to obey Allah, let him obey Him; whoever vows to disobey Allah, let him not disobey Him.' The same principle applies to oaths: one must not fulfill an oath that leads to sin. The passage then makes a distinction: the requirement for the oath to be 'limited by time' (muwaqqatah bi waqt) applies only to affirmative oaths (like 'I will do such-and-such'), not to negative oaths (like 'I will not pray'). In a negative oath involving sin (e.g., swearing not to pray), breaking the oath can occur before death by performing the prayer (i.e., breaking the oath is good). The hadith sources are cited at the end.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath; here, it is obligatory when the oath involves sin.
يمين مؤقتة (yamīn muwaqqatah): An oath limited by a specific time frame (e.g., 'I will do it today').

Types of Sworn Matters and Their Rulings
Classifies sworn matters and determines action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sworn matter into one of three categories and state the preferred action (obligatory, recommended, or better to keep the oath) based on the Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Breaking an oath is not always a sin; its ruling depends on the nature of the sworn matter.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq teaches us that the ruling on breaking an oath varies. It is not a simple binary of permissible or forbidden. By analyzing the sworn matter relative to its opposite, we can determine whether breaking is obligatory, recommended, or better avoided. The author relies on a famous hadith and then applies it to three categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith proof
The author begins with the hadith that if you see something better than what you swore, do that better thing and expiate the oath. This hadith is the basis for the classification.
The hadith is from Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Distinguish the three types
The sworn matter is compared to its opposite. It may be a sin, its opposite may be better, or they may be equal. Each leads to a different ruling.
A fourth type (obligatory sworn matter) is also noted.
3
Determine the ruling for each type
For sin, breaking is obligatory. For opposite better, breaking is recommended. For equal, preserving is better.
For obligatory sworn matter, preserving is mandatory.
4
Understand the practical application
Examples given: swearing not to pray (sin) -> must break; swearing not to cohabit for a month (opposite better) -> breaking recommended; swearing not to eat bread (equal) -> keep oath.
The ruling is not absolute but context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان فأيت غيرها خيراً منها فائت الذي هو خير وكفر عن يمينك (۱) ثم اليمين في الحديث بمعنى المقسم عليه لأن حقيقة اليمين جملتان إحداهما مقسم به والأخرى مقسم عليه فذكر الكل وأريد البعض، وقيل ذكر اسم الحال وأريد المحل لأن المحلوف عليه محل اليمين ولأن فيما قلناه تفويت البر إلى جابر وهو الكفارة ولا جابر للمعصية في ضده. وأطلق في المعصية فشمل النفي والإثبات فالأول مثل أن لا يصلي أو لا يكلم أباه فيجب الحنث بالصلاة وكلام الأب، والثاني نحو ليقتلن فلاناً كما في الهداية ولا بد أن تكون اليمين موقتة بوقت كاليوم وغداً لأنها لو كانت مطلقة لم يتصور الحنث باختياره لأنه لا يحنث إلا في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته فيوصي بالكفارة حينئذ إذا هلك الحالف ويكفر عن يمينه إذا هلك المحلوف عليه ؛ كذا في غاية البيان الثاني أن يكون المحلوف عليه شيئاً غيره أولى منه كالحلف على ترك وطء زوجته شهراً أو نحوه فالحنث أفضل لأن الرفق أيمن ودليله الحديث المتقدم. وكذا لو حلف ليضربن عبده، وهو يستأهل ذلك أو ليشكون مديونه إن لم يوافه غداً لأن العفو أفضل، وكذا تيسير المطالبة الثالث أن يحلف على شيء وضده مثله كالحلف لا يأكل هذا الخبز أو لا يلبس هذا الثوب فالبر في هذا وحفظ اليمين أولى ولو قال قائل إنه واجب لقوله تعالى واحفظوا أيمانكم [المائدة : ۸۹] على ما هو المختار في تأويلها أنه البر فيها أمكن؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ولم يذكر القسم الرابع وهو أن يكون المحلوف عليه يجب فعله؛ قيل اليمين كحلفه ليصلين الظهر اليوم لظهور أن البر فرض ومنه إذا كان المحلوف عليه ترك معصية فإن البر واجب فيثبت وجوبان لأمرين الفعل والبر. فحاصله أن المحلوف عليه إما فعل أو ترك وكل منهما على خمسة أوجه لأنه إما أن يكون معصية أو واجباً أو هو أولى من غيره أو غيره أولى منه أو مستويان وقد علمت أحكام العشرة
The Book of Oaths. 'If you see other than it better than it, then come to that which is better and expiate your oath.' Then 'yamin' in the hadith means the sworn matter, because the reality of an oath is two sentences: one is the swearing by, the other is the sworn matter. So the whole is mentioned but part is intended. It is also said the name of the state is mentioned and the place is intended, because the sworn matter is the place of the oath. And because in what we said, leaving virtue requires a compensator, which is the expiation, and there is no compensator for sin in its opposite. He spoke generally about sin, covering both negative and positive. The first is like swearing not to pray or not to speak to his father; then breaking the oath by praying and speaking to the father is obligatory. The second is like swearing 'by Allah I will kill so-and-so' as in al-Hidayah; and the oath must be limited to a time like today or tomorrow, because if it were absolute, breaking the oath voluntarily could not be imagined, since he only breaks it in the last moment of his life; then he gives instructions for expiation when he dies, and his oath is expiated when the one sworn about dies. The second type is that the sworn matter is something whose opposite is better, like swearing not to have intercourse with his wife for a month or so; then breaking the oath is better because gentleness is more auspicious; its proof is the aforementioned hadith. Similarly, if he swears to beat his slave (and he deserves it) or to sue his debtor unless he comes tomorrow, then forgiveness is better, and similarly easing the demand for payment. The third type is that he swears about something and its opposite are equal, like swearing not to eat this bread or not to wear this garment; then fulfilling the oath and preserving it is better. If someone says it is obligatory due to the verse 'And protect your oaths' (5:89) according to the chosen interpretation that it means fulfilling them, it is possible. And he did not mention the fourth type, namely that the sworn matter is obligatory to do, like swearing to pray Zuhr today, because clearly fulfilling is obligatory; and similarly if the sworn matter is leaving a sin, then fulfillment is obligatory; thus two obligations arise: the act and fulfillment. In summary, the sworn matter is either an act or an omission, each of five types: it may be a sin, obligatory, better than its opposite, its opposite is better, or equal. You have learned the rulings of the ten cases.
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, classifies sworn matters and determines the preferred action in each case. The author begins by quoting the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'If you see something better than what you swore about, then do that better thing and expiate your oath.' This hadith establishes that breaking an oath is not always wrong; it can be commendable. The commentary explains that the word 'yamin' (oath) in the hadith refers to the sworn matter itself, not the swearing act. Then the passage categorizes the sworn matter into three types based on its nature relative to its opposite: (1) When the sworn matter is a sin (ma'siyah), breaking the oath is obligatory (wajib) because there is no expiation for sin. Examples include swearing not to pray or not to speak to one's father. (2) When the opposite of the sworn matter is better, breaking the oath is recommended (afdal). Examples include swearing not to have intercourse with one's wife for a month (where breaking is better for marital harmony) or swearing to beat a slave (where forgiveness is better). (3) When the sworn matter and its opposite are equal, preserving the oath is better. The passage also mentions a fourth type not discussed in the original text: when the sworn matter is itself obligatory, then fulfillment is mandatory. Overall, the lesson provides a nuanced Hanafi view that balances oath-keeping with higher legal objectives.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): In this passage, 'yamin' refers to both the act of swearing and the sworn matter itself, but the author specifies it is used here to mean the sworn matter (al-muqsam 'alayh).
حنث (hanth): The violation or breaking of an oath by doing the opposite of what was sworn.
بر (birr): Fulfilling the oath by doing what was sworn or refraining from it as promised.
كفارة (kaffara): The expiation required for breaking a valid oath, which is feeding or clothing ten poor persons, or freeing a slave, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Swearing not to speak to one's father, then speaking to him
B) Swearing to beat a slave who deserves it, then forgiving him
C) Swearing not to eat a piece of bread, then eating it
D) Swearing to pray Zuhr today, then not praying

Key Takeaway
The ruling on breaking an oath depends on whether the sworn matter is a sin, its opposite is better, or both are equal; breaking can be wajib, afdal, or better to avoid.

The Oath of a Disbeliever: Invalid and No Kaffara
Ruling on disbeliever's oath with madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the oath of a disbeliever, distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, and apply the Hanafi interpretation of Quran 9:12 to the case of an apostate's oath.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath is invalid—no kaffara because their oath lacks legal reality and eligibility.

You may wonder whether a non-Muslim's oath carries any weight in Islamic law. The Hanafi school holds that such an oath is fundamentally invalid, and breaking it requires no expiation (kaffara). This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this ruling, the scriptural proof from Quran 9:12, and the key disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. You will learn not only the ruling but also how the Hanafi jurists defend their interpretation through both textual evidence and rational argument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by clearly stating that according to the Hanafi school, a disbeliever's oath is not a valid shar'i oath (yamin). Consequently, if broken, it incurs no kaffara. Note that this applies even if the person later becomes Muslim and then breaks the oath.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. He interprets Quran 9:12 ("They have no oaths") to mean that disbelievers do not fulfill their oaths, not that their oaths are invalid. For him, the oath itself is valid and carries the obligation of kaffara if broken.
3
Follow the Shafi'i proof
Examine the Shafi'i evidence: the same verse, plus the practice of the Prophet making Jews swear oaths in court, which suggests that their oaths have some legal effect.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi response refutes this: the second part of the verse ("they broke their oaths") refers only to the form (surah) of oaths, not the legal reality. The Prophet's action was merely to elicit a confession or refusal, not to treat the oath as binding. Additionally, a rational argument (fiqhi) is made: eligibility for an oath implies eligibility for kaffara; a disbeliever is not eligible for kaffara, so the oath is invalid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: if a disbeliever swears an oath, then becomes Muslim, and then breaks it, the Hanafi ruling is no kaffara; the Shafi'i ruling would be that kaffara is required. The same applies if a Muslim apostatizes and later returns: the original oath is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا) كفارة على كافر وإن حنث مسلماً) لما قدمنا أن شرط انعقادها الإسلام لأنه ليس بأهل لليمين لأنها تعقد لتعظيم الله تعالى ومع الكفر لا يكون معظماً ولا هو للكفارة أهل ودليله قوله تعالى إنهم لا أيمان لهم ﴾ [التوبة : ١٢] وأما قوله بعده نكثوا أيمانهم [التوبة : ۱۲] فيعني صورة الأيمان التي أظهروها . والحاصل أنه لا بد من التأويل وأما في لا أيمان لهم كما قال الشافعي إن المراد لا إيفاء لهم بها، أو في نكثوا أيمانهم على قول أبي حنيفة أن المراد ما هو صورة الأيمان دون حقيقتها الشرعية، ويرجح الثاني بالفقه وهو أنا نعلم من كان أهلاً لليمين يكون أهلاً للكفارة وليس الكافر أهلاً لها. أطلقه فشمل المرتد وأشار المصنف إلى أن الكفر يبطل اليمين فلو حلف مسلماً ثم ارتد والعياذ بالله تعالى ثم أسلم ثم حنث لا يلزمه شيء بعد الإسلام ولا قبله. قالوا : ولو نذر الكافر بما هو قربة لا يلزمه شيء، وأما تحليفه القاضي وقوله عليه السلام تبرئكم يهود بخمسين يميناً فالمراد كما قلنا صورة الأيمان فإن المقصود منها رجاء النكول لأنه يعتقد في نفسه تعظيم اسم الله تعالى وإن كان لا يقبل منه ولا يثاب عليه وهو المراد بقولهم ومع الكفر لا يكون معظماً.
His statement: 'And no kaffara is due from a disbeliever, even if he breaks the oath after becoming Muslim' – because we have already established that the condition for the validity of an oath is Islam; since he is not eligible for an oath because it is made to glorify Allah, and with unbelief he is not glorifying, nor is he eligible for kaffara. Its proof is the Quranic verse: 'They have no oaths' (9:12). As for His subsequent statement 'they broke their oaths' (9:12), it means the form of oaths that they manifested. In summary, interpretation is necessary. As for 'no oaths for them', as al-Shafi'i said, it means no fulfillment of them; or in 'they broke their oaths', according to Abu Hanifa, it means what is the form of oaths without their true legal nature. The second is preferred by fiqh: we know that whoever is eligible for an oath is eligible for kaffara, and the disbeliever is not eligible for it. This is general, including the apostate. The author indicated that disbelief invalidates the oath: if one swore while Muslim, then apostatized (may Allah protect us), then became Muslim again, then broke the oath, nothing is required of him after Islam or before. They said: if a disbeliever makes a vow (nadhr) to do something good, nothing is required. As for the judge making him swear, and the Prophet's saying 'the Jews will clear you with fifty oaths', it means, as we said, the form of oaths; the purpose is hope for refusal because he believes in himself the glorification of Allah's name, even if it is not accepted from him and he is not rewarded for it. This is what is meant by their statement: 'with disbelief he is not glorifying.'
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the validity of oaths made by a disbeliever. The Hanafi position is clear: a disbeliever's oath is not a valid shar'i oath (yamin) and therefore breaking it does not require kaffara. The proof is the Quranic verse 'They (the polytheists) have no oaths' (9:12). The commentator addresses an objection from Imam al-Shafi'i, who interprets the verse to mean that they do not fulfill their oaths, not that their oaths are invalid. The Hanafi response favors Abu Hanifa's interpretation that the 'oaths' mentioned in the second part of the verse refer to the mere form of oaths (surah al-ayman) without their legal reality. This is supported by a rational argument (fiqhi): if a person were eligible for oaths, they would be eligible for kaffara; but a disbeliever is not eligible for kaffara, so their oath is invalid. The ruling extends to apostates: if a Muslim swears an oath, then apostatizes, and later returns to Islam and breaks the oath, no kaffara is due because the oath was invalidated by the apostasy. Similarly, a disbeliever's vow (nadhr) to do a good deed is not binding. The passage also clarifies that when a judge makes a disbeliever swear (e.g., in court), it is only the form of an oath, not a valid legal oath; the purpose is to induce confession or refusal.

Key Terms
كافر (kafir): A person who does not believe in Allah and the Prophet Muhammad; the text specifies that such a person is not eligible (ahl) for oaths or kaffara.
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate from Islam. Included in the general ruling; apostasy invalidates an oath, so no kaffara upon return and violation.
صورة اليمين (surah al-yamin): The external form or wording of an oath without its legal reality (haqiqah shar'iyyah). Used to explain the swearing of disbelievers in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a disbeliever swears an oath and then breaks it after converting to Islam?
A) He must pay kaffara because the oath was valid and binding
B) He must pay kaffara only if he broke it before converting
C) He does not pay kaffara because his original oath was invalid
D) He must repeat the oath and then pay kaffara

Key Takeaway
Being Muslim at the time of an oath is a condition for its validity; a disbeliever's oath is void, and breaking it never triggers kaffara, whether before or after conversion.

Tahrim al-Halal: The Ruling on Declaring Lawful Things as Unlawful
States that declaring halal as haram does not make it haram but requires kaffara if violated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making halal things haram, identify the Quranic proof, and distinguish the majority view from the minority intra-school opinion.

Core Idea
Declaring halal as haram does not change its status; it creates an oath requiring kaffara if violated.

In this lesson, we examine a common scenario: a person declares something lawful (like food or clothing) as 'haram' on themselves. Does this actually make the thing forbidden? The Hanafi school says no – but there is a legal consequence. Drawing on Surah al-Tahrim, the scholars treat such statements as implied oaths. If the person then uses the thing, they must pay kaffara (expiation). This ruling has many practical applications, from food to speech to property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The passage begins: 'Whoever declares his property as haram, it does not become haram.' Understand that the act itself does not change the legal status; only Allah changes lawful/unlawful.
This is the core Hanafi principle: human declaration cannot alter divine law.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The author cites Quran 66:1-2. Note the phrase 'tahillat aymanikum' – the expiation of your oaths. This shows that the Prophet's prohibition of something halal was treated as an oath, not an actual prohibition.
The verse is qati al-thubut and qati al-dalala in Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'tahillah' (expiation) indicates that declaring halal haram is equivalent to an oath. If it were an actual prohibition, expiation would not be prescribed. Thus, the ruling: such a statement is a yamin (oath) that requires kaffara if violated.
This is the key istinbat from the source.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
The passage mentions a difference: does eating a part of a declared substance constitute violation? The majority (from al-Khulasa, al-Muhit) say yes – even a mouthful triggers kaffara. A minority (Fatawa Qadikhan) says it is like an explicit oath not to eat the whole, so partial eating does not violate.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: any consumption (hanth) with istimta' requires kaffara.
5
Apply to practical examples
Work through the scenarios: speech (saying 'your speech is haram on me' – violated when spoken to), food (loaf – any morsel), clothing (wearing), dirhams (buying violates, charity does not because it is not personal enjoyment). Understand that 'use' means any form of personal enjoyment (istimta').
Charity or gift does not violate because the prohibition is on personal benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ومن حرم ملكه لم يحرم) أي لا يصير حراماً عليه لذاته لأنه قلب المشروع وتغييره ولا قدرة له على ذلك بل الله تعالى هو المتصرف في ذلك بالتبديل، وغيره إن استباحه كفر أي عامله معاملة المباح بأن فعل ما . رمه الله فإنه يلزمه كفارة اليمين لقوله تعالى يا أيها النبي لم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ﴾ [التحريم: ١] الآيتين. فبين الله تعالى أن نبيه عليه السلام حرم شيئاً مما هو حلال وأنه فرض له تحلته فعبر عن ذلك بقوله «تحلة أيمانكم» فعلم أن تحريم الحلال يمين موجب للكفارة وما في بعض الروايات من أنه يحلف صريحاً فليس هو في الآية ولا في الحديث الصحيح إلى آخر ما في فتح القدير . ولو ذكر المصنف بدل الملك الشيء بأن قال ومن حرم شيئاً ثم فعله كفر لكان أولى ليشمل الأعيان والأفعال وملكه وملك غيره وما كان حلالاً وما كان حراماً فيدخل فيه ما إذا قال كلامك علي حرام أو معي أو الكلام معك حرام كما في المبتغى . كذا إذا قال دخول هذا المنزل علي حرام ونحوه كما في المجتبى. ولو قال لقوم كلامكم علي حرام أيهم كلم حنث . وفي مجموع النوازل: وكذا كلام فلان وفلان علي حرام يحنث بكلام أحدهما، وكذا كلام أهل بغداد وكذا أكل هذا الرغيف علي حرام يحنث بأكل لقمة بخلاف ما لو قال والله لا أكلمهم لا يحنث حتى يكلمهم. وفي الخلاصة : لو قال هذا الرغيف علي حرام حنث بأكل لقمة. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان قال مشايخنا: الصحيح أنه لا يكون حانثاً لأن قوله هذا الرغيف علي حرام بمنزلة قوله والله لا أكل هذا الرغيف، ولو قال هكذا لم يحنث بأكل البعض ا هـ . مع أن حرمة العين والمراد منها تحريم الفعل فإذا قال هذا الطعام علي حرام فالمراد أكله، وكذا إذا قال هذا الثوب علي حرام فالمراد لبسه إلا إذا نوى غيره كما في الخلاصة : ولو قال لدراهم في يده هذ الدراهم علي حرام إن اشترى بها حنث وإن تصدق بها أو وهبها لم يحنث بحكم العرف كما في المحيط وغيره، ولا خصوصية للدراهم بل لو وهب ما جعله حراماً أو تصدق به لم يحنث لأن المراد بالتحريم حرمة الاستمتاع. وفي المحيط : لو قال مالي علي حرام فأنفق منه شيئاً حنث، وكذا مال فلان علي حرام فأكل منه أو أنفق حنث، ويدخل فيه ما إذا قال هذا الطعام علي حرام لطعام لا قوله : (وعبر المصنف بمن الخ) قال في النهر : أنت خبير بأن في شمول كلامه لذلك نظيراً بيناً
His statement: 'And whoever declares his property as haram, it does not become haram' – meaning it does not become forbidden for him in itself, because that would be a change of the divine law and he has no power to do so; rather Allah alone controls that by substitution. But if he uses it, he treats it as permissible, that is, he does an act that Allah has forbidden; then the kaffara of an oath becomes necessary, due to His statement: 'O Prophet, why do you prohibit that which Allah has made lawful to you?' (66:1) and the two verses. Allah clarified that His Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited something which was halal and obligated an expiation for him, and expressed that by saying 'the expiation of your oaths.' So it is known that making halal unlawful is an oath that necessitates kaffara. As for some narrations that he explicitly swore, that is not in the verse or authentic hadith, as in Fath al-Qadir. If the author had said 'whoever prohibits something' instead of 'property', it would be better to include entities and actions, one's own property and others', halal and haram. So it includes if he says 'your speech is haram on me' or 'my speech is haram on you' etc. Similarly, if he says 'entering this house is haram on me' etc. If he says to a group 'your speech is haram on me', whoever speaks, he violates. In Majmu' al-Nawazil: similarly 'the speech of so-and-so is haram on me' – he violates by the speech of one of them. Similarly, 'the speech of the people of Baghdad' and 'eating this loaf is haram on me' – he violates by eating a mouthful, unlike if he said 'By Allah I will not speak to them' – he does not violate until he speaks to them. In al-Khulasa: if he says 'this loaf is haram on me', he violates by eating a mouthful. In Fatawa Qadikhan: our scholars say the correct view is that he is not a violator because his statement 'this loaf is haram on me' is equivalent to his saying 'By Allah I will not eat this loaf', and if he said thus he would not violate by eating part. So the prohibition of the substance means prohibition of the action; if he says 'this food is haram on me', the intent is eating it. Similarly, if he says 'this garment is haram on me', the intent is wearing it, unless he intended something else. In al-Khulasa: if he says of dirhams in his hand 'these dirhams are haram on me', he violates if he buys with them, but if he gives them as charity or gifts he does not violate, by custom. There is no specificity for dirhams; indeed if he gives as charity what he made haram, he does not violate because the intent of prohibition is prohibition of enjoyment. In al-Muhit: if he says 'my wealth is haram on me' and spends something from it, he violates; similarly 'so-and-so's wealth is haram on me' – if he eats or spends from it, he violates. This includes if he says 'this food is haram on me' for food... His statement: (And the author expressed with 'man' etc.) said in al-Nahr: you are well aware that the inclusion of his speech is a clear parallel.
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This section explains the ruling when a person declares something halal as haram on themselves, such as saying 'this food is haram on me.' The Hanafi ruling is that such a declaration does not actually make the thing forbidden (haram) by itself, because only Allah has the authority to make things lawful or unlawful. However, it is treated as an implied oath (yamin). If the person then uses the thing (e.g., eats the food), they must pay kaffara for breaking an oath. This is based on the Quranic verses in Surah al-Tahrim (66:1-2) where the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited something lawful, and Allah prescribed an expiation, calling it 'the expiation of your oaths.' The passage then gives many examples to illustrate the concept. For instance, if a person says 'your speech is haram on me,' he breaks the oath by speaking to them. For substances like a loaf of bread, the prohibition is on the act of eating; even a morsel constitutes a violation. There is a difference of opinion quoted from Fatawa Qadikhan: some scholars say that saying 'this loaf is haram' is like saying 'I swear I will not eat it,' so eating a part does not break the oath until the whole is eaten. But the majority view in the Hanafi school is that any consumption of the substance breaks the oath. The passage also discusses specific cases: if someone declares their dirhams haram, buying with them breaks the oath, but giving them as charity does not, because the intended prohibition is on personal enjoyment, not absolute disposition. Thus, the key concept is that 'tahrim al-halal' (making lawful unlawful) is a legal trick (hila) that creates an oath obligation, not an actual change in the legal status of the thing.

Key Terms
تحريم الحلال (tahrim al-halal): The act of declaring something permissible as forbidden on oneself, which is treated as an implied oath (yamin) in Hanafi fiqh.
تحلة الأيمان (tahillat al-ayman): The expiation of oaths mentioned in the Quran (66:2), referring to the kaffara for breaking an oath.
حنث (hanth): Violation of the implied oath by using the thing declared haram, e.g., eating, wearing, spending.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, what is the consequence of declaring something halal as 'haram on me' and then using it?
A) The thing becomes haram permanently.
B) Nothing happens because the declaration is invalid.
C) Kaffara of an oath becomes wajib.
D) The person must renew their iman.

Key Takeaway
Declaring halal as haram is an implied oath; using the thing requires kaffara, but the thing remains halal.

Oaths of a Disbeliever: No Binding Effect
Ruling on oaths of disbelievers and apostates

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a disbeliever's oath does not obligate kaffarah, and explain the procedural purpose of administering oaths to non-Muslims.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath does not obligate expiation, but a judge may use it to induce nukul for procedural effect.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the legal status of oaths made by disbelievers and apostates. It addresses two key points: first, that such oaths carry no religious obligation—no kaffarah is required even if broken after embracing Islam; second, that a judge may still make a disbeliever swear an oath in court, not for religious binding, but to encourage nukul (refusal) which can lead to a judgment. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between the spiritual invalidity of the oath and its potential procedural utility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage begins with a person who was Muslim, apostatized, then returned to Islam, and later broke an oath. The ruling: no kaffarah is required, neither before nor after his return.
Note: The oath taken during apostasy is not considered valid for expiation.
2
Understand the underlying principle
A disbeliever's oath is not religiously binding because the oath-taker lacks the requisite faith to make the oath effective. Thus no expiation is incurred.
3
Distinguish between religious and procedural oaths
The judge may still administer an oath to a disbeliever for procedural purposes—specifically, the hope that the person will refuse (nukul) out of reverence for God's name, leading to a judgment against them. This is not a religious obligation.
The oath has no spiritual value but may have legal consequences in court.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The Prophet's statement 'The Jews will make you take an oath with fifty oaths' shows that oaths were used for procedural truth-seeking, not as religious acts binding for kaffarah.
The hadith is cited to support the permissibility of administering oaths to non-Muslims in court.
5
Apply the concept of nukul
Nukul (refusal to swear) is the desired outcome because the disbeliever may still revere Allah's name in his heart, even though the oath is not accepted from him. This fear of God can lead to truth or default judgment.
The statement 'with disbelief, he is not considered to be glorifying' means the act of swearing does not count as worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلماً ثم ارتد والعياذ بالله تعالى ثم أسلم ثم حنث لا يلزمه شيء بعد الإسلام ولا قبله. قالوا : ولو نذر الكافر بما هو قربة لا يلزمه شيء، وأما تحليفه القاضي وقوله عليه السلام تبرئكم يهود بخمسين يميناً فالمراد كما قلنا صورة الأيمان فإن المقصود منها رجاء النكول لأنه يعتقد في نفسه تعظيم اسم الله تعالى وإن كان لا يقبل منه ولا يثاب عليه وهو المراد بقولهم ومع الكفر لا يكون معظماً.
A Muslim then apostatized (God forbid), then became Muslim, then broke his oath: nothing is required from him after Islam nor before it. They said: If a disbeliever vows something that is an act of worship, nothing is required from him. As for the judge making him swear and the Prophet's saying: 'The Jews will make you take an oath with fifty oaths,' the meaning is, as we said, the form of oaths, because the purpose is hope of refusal (nukul), since he believes in his heart in the majesty of the name of Allah, even though it is not accepted from him nor is he rewarded. That is the meaning of their statement: 'And with disbelief, he is not considered to be glorifying [Allah].'
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal status of oaths taken by disbelievers and apostates within the Hanafi school. It begins with a case: a person who was Muslim apostatized, then returned to Islam, and subsequently broke an oath. The ruling is that no expiation (kaffarah) is required, neither before nor after his return to Islam. This is because the oath taken during apostasy or disbelief is not valid in a way that obligates kaffarah. Similarly, if a disbeliever makes a vow (nadhr) to perform an act of worship, it is not binding. The passage then explains the practice of a judge making a disbeliever swear an oath, as indicated by the hadith that the Jews were made to swear fifty oaths. The purpose is to encourage the oath-taker to refuse (nukul) out of reverence for the divine name, even though the oath of a disbeliever is not accepted spiritually and does not yield reward. The statement 'with disbelief, he is not considered to be glorifying' underscores that the disbeliever's act of swearing does not carry the same religious weight.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take an oath, which is hoped for when a disbeliever is made to swear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a disbeliever who makes a vow (nadhr) to perform an act of worship?
A) It is binding and he must fulfill it if he becomes Muslim.
B) Nothing is required from him.
C) He must pay kaffarah if he breaks it.
D) It is valid only if he intends it as an oath.

Key Takeaway
A disbeliever's oath or vow does not obligate any kaffarah, but a judge may still administer oaths to encourage nukul for procedural purposes.
